RATING: 9
I'm going to declare this as my first ever 10 on BGG!  Which will also make this my #1 game and the game I am always wanting to play.  Would also like to try it with 4-5 players to see how it scales and plays out on the bigger board.  So much depth, yet so simple to play and everything just makes sense and plays out very well!
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
I understand where others say "it's a drafting game that you don't know what you're building towards" which I think holds a large amount of truth in it in terms of how to approach the initial 1/3rd of the game (if not longer) with the accumulation of workers for your economic engine of sorts. There is probably 1/3rd of the game where you're focused on cards and getting started on the board, but determining what to do there falls out of a co-dependency with other players (as does when to have that switch in focus and balance). There is a certain amount of an engine building or a workforce management that you work on at the beginning and then there is this transition into board focused spatial play. In that regard, it sort of reminds me of a more complicated Lewis & Clark with staffing and card usage each turn with the spatial piece of The Great Zimbabwe added in and a slathering of generic euro engine building layed on top. One area that it different from TGZ is there seems to be a certain amount of runaway/snowball effect that the milestones provoke and the underlying question is "who can runaway faster" as the end post you're reaching for doesn't change, so delaying getting a milestone isn't to your benefit if you're going to try and incorporate it into your strategy. Conversely, because anyone who meets a milestone's condition that turn receives it, there is a dominant pathway of recruitment that provides for an advantage..

Compared to Indonesia (the other heavy econ Splotter I have), this feels slightly more tactical, much more euro-y (build some semblance of an engine, crank it as hard as possible, try and run away with it, etc), less permanent (due to how marketing happens and things sort of turn quickly as a result of changed demands and a better ability to forecast what's coming) and less opaque in it's decision analysis. I would say it's less opaque as well in terms of just saying "well is this a good idea or no?" By no means is it a simple game, just a general comparison of the two titles (not all of that is bad, just different). 

Conversely, the plethora of things you can do is almost dizzying in a wonderful way, even if there are two areas of strategic pivoting (once at the onset of the game, and again after about 8 turns when everyone is up and running for a bit). It is much more dynamic with price cutting/raising and changing demand via marketing expiration piece make for a really interesting twist on interaction, and I find it more interactive than either Indonesia or Roads & Boats. Slightly stronger 7 than Roads & Boats (primarily due to interaction and play duration), not nearly as interesting to me as Indonesia (which I say is a 9). It rises above "generic heavy euro" but not by much.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9.5
Our favorite game of 2015. We couldn't stop playing, it's hugely compulsive.  Even yesterday we played 3 games one after the other (each lasting about 2 hours with 3 players and $800-900 in the reserve). I'm already looking forward to our next play.

There are so many options - in our very first game we found it slightly daunting and difficult to know which way to jump: there are a huge number of combinations of staff and milestones to consider. But one game is enough to give you a grasp on the roles available and the significance of the milestone bonuses.

The randomly generated map and the dynamic nature of the marketing campaigns and property development means you get a different game experience each time and opportunities to try out different strategies. But it is certainly the case that bad choices made in the first couple of turns could see you struggle for the whole rest of the game.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9.5
Lots of intro games played, have yet to go on to play a full game in person, but can't wait to. We're just getting started with this one! 

A surprisingly easy to pick up economic game with a great theme and lots of depth. The wide array of recruitment options gives a sandboxy feel and you can get quite creative with strategies. Later on things get more involved with more demands flying around and people's rosters growing, but it never feels bogged down. 

A modern classic, could climb higher.

edit: the full game is very satisfying indeed, can't wait to play more.

RATING: 6
Finally got the chance to try this game. It's just not for me. The design is too harsh & unforgiving. This is a game where the more experienced player is guaranteed to win, and I understand some enjoy that, but I wasn't particularly thrilled with that. You can literally lose this game on the first turn (...and every turn after that), but you continue playing. Additionally, the game is extremely hostile and I felt there was no way for anyone to get ahead without harming another player. Most of the game comes down to mathematical calculations, which I found rather dull. That said, it's a well-designed, excellent game. I'd recommend it to the right person and I understand why so many folks love it. The low rating is based on how hard it'd be to get me personally to play the game again & how little I enjoy it; it's not a reflection of whether I think it is a good game (which I think it is).

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
2.5 plays / 3,4 players (0.5 Intro play)

LIKE: Deep, deep game. Interesting theme. I enjoyed the first-to-get milestones. I also enjoyed how marketing was used to game demand, based on area and duration, it added some interesting aspects.

DISLIKE: Can be cutthroat, and players can get shut out to the point of being eliminated, because better efficiency from an opponent can cause a downward spiral for other players. It's a medium-long game, but manageable with 3 players. Fiddly towards the end, moving tokens and counting cash. Can be a table hog. Employee combos need to be known to play well against experienced players.

OVERALL: A good game, but I feel the all-or-nothing aspect of food deliveries is too punishing for this length of a game. I wish some of the bookkeeping was smoother, the CFO slows the game way down. I like The Great Zimbabwe better because of the shorter setup time and smaller table space. Getting efficient trainers seemed almost essential for a long game, but I do like to see if there's alternative strategies.

RATING: 6
A deep game that does not disappoint strategy and theme wise as usual from Splotter but this is probably the best game in my experience that they have produced. Not only does everything immerse you into the idea of running a food chain but the strategy you go down can sink you just like in real life if one lacks the ability to adapt to a changing market. The rules are the biggest surprise as usually Splotter games are very complex to teach but this game was quick and easy to learn for anyone of a regular boardgaming level. And the way of choosing facedown by each player the length of the game by the variable amount of money in the bank needed to exhaust to end the game adds a whole new level to strategy than just a standard amount of turns. Also, running a food chain is a unique theme that is fun to roleplay if one is so inclined. 

Downsides is the game can be brutal and one can find a downward spiral of dropping behind the leader if a decision was not thought out far enough. The game also takes a while to play, 2-4hrs depending on player count. 

In conclusion, a great game with immense theme and strategy to boot. Can't recommend it enough with it's cool theme and fun gameplay.

RATING: 9
Wow!  What a great game!  It's an interesting exploration of supply/demand.  One unique element is that players must provide or establish both the supply AND the demand.  The tech tree of the employees creates a number of additional strategies and opportunities that interlace with the supply/demand underpinning.  This leads to some complicated interactions and extremely provocative timing considerations since there are inherent delays in most everything (especially the demand side).

One criticism is the play time and that's pretty inherent to the overall engine.  I'd be reluctant to engage in a 5-player enterprise, though one key part of the turn is simultaneous and many other parts are automated (still, players will respond to other's actions, leading to the potential for significant delays).

Another criticism is the components.  Rather low production value for the price, but then it is a Splotter and the game is more than good enough to get over this hurdle.

I have a few longer, heavier games that I absolutely love and it tends to be the game length that is prohibitive in getting them to the table.  I will pick up a copy of Food Chain Magnate and I expect it will fall into this category.  It's an excellent euro that you really should try.

RATING: 10
One of my favourite games. Vastly variable set-up although the opening strategies can get a bit limited as you gain in experience, it's very open-ended once you get going.

Try and stay at the same experience level as your play group or you might find you outclass them and it's difficult to handicap. There's no catch-up mechanism and it's not terribly forgiving for the inexperienced or if you make a mistake. If everyone is playing at the same level however it's a fantastic game for all involved.

RATING: 5
I really wanted to like this game, and I did.  At least until half way through the game when we discovered that the game had a huge and utterly ridiculous design flaw, which entirely ruined it.

It's possible to spend half of the game building a perfectly good engine for making money, only to have someone - in a single move - completely destroy that engine for the game.

This isn't just bad planning, mistakes or bad luck - this is just a basic flaw in the design of the game.  

In our very first play, we had a situation where a player was competitive for the first half of the game - but one move by another player just killed the game for that player (and it wasn't even remotely aimed at hurting the player), who then spent the second half (about an hour and a half) of the game losing money (no matter what they did) and it was really obvious as soon as it happened that this was going to be the case.

Apologists for this horribly overrated game will argue that you can avoid this situation [i]once you know about it[/i], but you shouldn't have to [i]plan[/i] to avoid a clear flaw in the game.

Long games with such a huge flaw should be avoided like the plague...

RATING: 9
On the box it says 100% dice free, which is exactly why this game appealed to me. The only randomness in this game is during setup, which can be quite random and lead to some very interesting scenarios. Once you've gotten over this bit of chaos the only other factor that can destroy your plans are your opponents. It is one of the most competitive games I've played. There is a big difference to being an experienced and a new player in this, as new players are bound to make oversights in a game that has so many equally important decisions.

This game is not a 10 to me as it is simply too heavy and too long to get to the table regularly. Which is a shame as I adore it to bits.

RATING: 6.5

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
While there is an economic element to this game, the logistic and drafting elements are the most defining and influential ones. This is a game about creating demand and fulfilling that demand to earn money, which is essentially victory points. To do both things efficiently, players must draft and upgrade their employees, which are represented by cards. But that is only half of the puzzle... the other half lies with the abstract/logistic nature of the game board, which dictates the access/distance between players' restaurants and the houses/vendors. 

Furthermore, this is a VERY interactive euro. There is no safe-place in Food Chain Magnate... players can undercut you, advertise products that you can't fulfill, and deny you access to high-tier employees. This is a game where a single mistake can have a domino effect where you end up firing your high-tier employees, which took you several turns to train. And there are no catch up mechanics in this 3+ hour game. 

And like other Splotter titles, this game is very low luck. In the beginning of each round, the cards/employees that players choose are done by simultaneous action selection. And while this may seem like rock-paper-scissors... in reality, players know what others are attempting as everything is drafted/upgraded openly. Furthermore, because high-tier employees cost salaries, it's rare that someone would have so much flexibility that you couldn't make an educated guess on what they would play. And even if they did, you can manipulate turn order by having empty employee slots.

The last element, which I really like about Food Chain Magnate, are the milestones. Basically, these are bonuses that are given to players who are first to accomplish one thing or another. And these bonuses become unavailable after the round in which they are first obtained. Functionally, these milestones create a focal point, which creates tension, and encourages player to adopt different strategies. 

Overall, this cut-throat interactive euro isn't going to be for everyone... but it is the type of game that I love to play. And perhaps most impressively, the game isn't that complicated compared to the meaty decisions it manages to create. This isn't a relaxing walk in the park... this is a walk in a mine field. 

That being said, the game isn't without criticism. From a mechanical point of view, there is little I can find fault in. The most notable criticism would be that the game can slow down with intense competition. But from a component point of view, Splotter has made some bewildering publishing decisions...
• The milestones should have never been printed on cards. It makes an already cluttered game even more cluttered.
• There is a good chance that goods will run out in the game. Food tracks should have been included. Furthermore, food tracks make the game less fiddly as players are often obtaining/discarding food. 
• The cards should have had iconography on the right/left borders so players can see them while splayed out. Trying to plan your turn, with 20+ some cards, is simply obnoxious without laying everything in the open (and of course, this is suppose to be done in secret). 

In the end, Splotter delivers substandard component quality at a huge premium. Many people defend their component quality by highlighting how rare/niche their games are. But with so many heavy euros coming out each year,... with much better components and lower prices... I don't quite buy it. Simply put, I think Splotter is stuck in the past, which is preventing their designs from reaching their full potential.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

A szerzők komplexitást tekintve nem hazudtolták meg magukat, bár ez könnyebb kicsit, mint az eddig általam próbált 2 játékuk. Egyébként ez az Indonesia-nál kicsit jobban tetszett a téma miatt, de az Antiquity-t nem veri.

AP veszély hatalmas, sorszáma van a házaknak, a kampányoknak (amik ez alapján következnek), számítanak a távolságok, bármikor le lehet rakni új éttermet, új házat, ami felborít mindent... 5 játékosnál mondjuk a 10-dik körben, amikor 30 kaja kerül fel körönként a keresleti meg kínálati oldalon is és mindenkinek van 20 embere... nem is folytatom:) Ja, és a felüljárók grafikája zavaró, mármint hogy nem lehet elmenni alattuk.

Akit nem riaszt az AP, és az hogy szívatós tud lenni a játék, annak ajánlom feltétlenül, tényleg nagyon jól össze van rakva, pár apró hibát leszámítva tök jó játék szerintem.

RATING: 8
Food Chain Magnate is an economic gamer's game about the fast food industry, set in the United States in the 50s.
It has two main mechanics: it is a drafting game, where each player must react to others' actions and the objective becomes manifest while playing; but it has also a very strong spatial element, conveyed by a modular board (giving each game a very different feel).
It has a steep learning curve (and mistakes are paid harshly: the game is very unforgiving!) and the downtime between turns can be quite long. Apart from these drawbacks, it is a very very solid economic game, with a sandbox approach. One of the best of the past year.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 9
I've played 4 player once and 2 player about 5 times so far. I find that the strategies are totally different, which makes playing with different numbers of people really interesting. Working out how to try and create demand for only one type of good so you can out supply your competitors is definitely difficult and the milestones change up the game a lot. I love the art style, it's beautiful and you can make so many jokes behind the art, little things like the women are the low level cooks and the men are the expert chefs. All the women are HR and all the Men are management, really encapsulates the 50s era.

I would recommend this for people who like resource management games with no chance elements and that have played board games before. This is not an entry level game. I think its best played with 3 or 4 as you essentially have to copy the player in a 2 player game or fall very far behind.

Sidenote: We felt we had to Nerf the Guru in a 2 player or the person who got him would dominate the game every time. We changed him to only be able to upgrade a single person twice but still have 3 possible upgrades.

RATING: 7.5
First Play: Watched the Shut up & Sit Down video and was surprised and encouraged when they said that the game mechanics were simple and quick to pick up. After my first game I must say that this impression is very misleading. Why?

Well OK there are some very simple and regimented steps to each turn but the options   are vast, first with regard to who to recruit and the branches that stem from those early decisions. Those decisions impact on the milestones that are achieved and they have knock on effects for cash for the rest of the game. It's fair to say that you can lose this game in the first 3-4 turns. 

As a result game #1 is a learning game. Gaining the global understanding of how it all hangs together took me 75% of the game. At that point I was earning $20 a turn whilst the experienced players were hauling in >$100. I suggest that a dummy round is a very good idea, maybe two, just to see how the milestones are earned and the importance of recruitment and training. 

I really want to play this again but this is a painful example of a game where experienced players will utterly wipe the floor with first timers. First timers need to be warned of this and given as much pre-game strategy advice as is feasible.

RATING: 9
A fantastic game about producing food to meet demand in your neighborhood. This is a game that isn't heavy on the rules but has so many different choices and different paths to victory that every turn matters. The game is brutal and so making a poor choice could set you back significantly possibly with no hope of ever coming back. It demands that you watch what other players are doing both on the board and with their employees and that you react appropriately. Stubbornly sticking to any single strategy will mean a guaranteed loss. The game just gives you the sandbox to play with and you and your fellow players are free to run your food empire however you like within it. 

An instant hit for me. The light rules, high interaction, multiple victory paths, and even the theme are just perfect. Due to the fact that the players really dictate how this game flows, that also means if you're playing a game with people of different experience levels it's going to really throw the balance of the game off. Not uncommon for bigger games but FCM seems like it would be especially prone to that. However, if you play this with other "serious" board gamers it's an awesome experience.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
Initial impressions:

Interesting game.  The most superficial complexity I've seen in a Splotter...ever, or perhaps on par with Antiquity.  This is probably a deeper game, which is a good thing.

Many aspects of other Splotters are present here, the most obvious being from TGZ, but major overlap also with Duck Dealer.  The hand management system would be better with a +1/+2/+N number at the base of the black cards, so you can simply mentally think 3+1+2+2=8 cards, rather than forcing a summation of N-1 approach - it's just an extra bit of overhead mental arithmetic when I'd rather focus on the game.  Similarly, either a player board with the milestones and cubes/squares to indicate which ones you have rather than a handful of cards which can be hard to see in a glance, as well as a board to track how many waitresses and price discounters and drinks/food a player could maximally play, would help a lot.  But perhaps the components for these would be cost-prohibitive.

I found the components and color palette absolutely gorgeous here, from the board to the menu to the card art style.  I'm really not sure what anyone has to complain about.  I've had some difficulty mapping restaurants to their owners however.

As for the game itself, there is a good deal going on - there are a lot of tempo plays and long-term strategic decisions early, which shifts to an almost entirely tactical game with few decisions for the last 3-4 turns as you are carried by the momentum of what you have chosen at the start.  It's all just posturing and trying to eek out that small edge over the competition at this point.

Milestones are incredibly important, some are so important that you can lose the game simply by not achieving one in time, depending on your focus.  I'm not sure how I ultimately feel about these, I'm never much of a fan of all-or-nothing mechanics.  It is the weakest part of the game for me.  I would rather see something where you the first to achieve gets the full bonus, then the next turn "half" the bonus, and finally it is removed.

Anyway, it is an interesting and exciting game which will warrant exploration for a dozen or so plays, at least.  Rating could climb to a 9, or could drop to a 6, depending on how my dissatisfaction with the milestones progresses over time.  Probably not going beneath a 4, probably not going to be in my top 10.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
I had been looking quite forward to playing this as it has achieved almost legendary status in heavy eurogame circles in no time at all.

Be aware that this game is a major tablehog! Be aware that this game is unforgiving! For instance, the guy who first gets to earn $100 get a free CFO, which increases his earning a further 50% on subsequent turns. No catch-up mechanisms here. If someone is pulling ahead you need to focus on eliminating his income possibilities.

I find it quite interesting that an economic game like this can be meaner than many of the ameritrashy games out there.

Anyway, this an extremely well designed game that plays differently to anything else I have tried. It is mechanically simple, but you have a ton of options and timing your actions properly is difficult and brainburny. Recommended for heavy gamers, but probably not for most other people.

Preliminary rating: 8

RATING: 7
This game is absolutely fantastic. And deceptively simple. Your hire some dudes, and then you play these dudes in a tree-like fashion in order to get every ability they allow. And if your the first to get some of these abilities played, you get extra special abilities that will change the way you play. That's it.

But then this game is all about reacting to what your friends are doing to the game. There's so many different ways to win it all but it always adjusts depending on the state of the board and what cards everyone has access to. Because of this, you end up watching everyone one pretty intensely and your always engaged - but your never waiting for long because turns are really short.

---

Bumped this down a couple ratings because I never get to play this anymore. It's just too long and the fun is VERY group-dependent...Some people just can't wrap their head around it. Coupled with the fact that this game is so punishing if your not good, this has lead to some real lackluster games.

So I'm at a conflicting point with this game. I love what this game is and can be with fellow players who are bright and engaged...But if it weren't so much on the rare and expensive side of things, I'd perhaps consider selling it

RATING: 1.75
Game design that makes me angry:
 - While it's an interesting twist to have players choose either a longer, medium or shorter game via hidden reserve cards, that's pointless because it's your first action in the game and can't be interacted later. You should not incorporate a Rock-Paper-Scissor mechanic in a long strategy game! You might guess the other players' reserve cards, but you have already had to commit to a certain build order. Some build orders can branch later, some can't. So you'd choose a build order that can branch, but there's one answer to every gamestate how you should branch so where's the game in that? It's more of a work situation, you shouldn't play by intuition. Game design that awards AP! Hurray!
 - The game is NOT interactive, but a race! It's just that some BOs (build orders) require certain other players to do something first to work (which you can't plan for). The seemingly tactical gameplay is overshadowed by the power of 1x employees and milestones, which are way more gamedeciding than careful tile-placement and opponent interaction.
 - The game is 100% open information. You can sit down and calculate your way to victory. You actually should. This makes it similar to chess, but without focus on tactical positioning, which is mostly irrelevant! What is the board for anyway? There's much added complexity without much gain.

RATING: 8.5
Awesome heavy strategy game. Not overly complex, with tons of choices that are not simply a tweak of familiar mechanics. The staff tree, with the ability to re-org every turn, is awesome. Very cut-throat as well, with the ability to completely lock out someone in a round depending on the cards played. You can leverage specific advertising to set demand and override competitors, pricing to cut out competitors, and the race for the powerful bonuses is awesome. 

Only knock is really the graphic design on the map tiles: totally seem like they are prototype components, especially for a game with the high price tag. Made of flimsy cardboard too, and the paper money is no better than Monopoly money. The menu rule pamphlets and the design on the cards though are great.

Can't wait to play again: can easily see this going to a 9 or higher.

Edit 08/13/16:  played three times now. Awesome, awesome game and bumped to a 9.  I think the only thing that will prevent this going higher is it is SOOO cut-throat. Discount 1 extra dollar over your rival? You can steal every sale and have a $200 swing no problem. At times the commensurate reward / punishment can outweigh the difference in strategic performance.

Edit 08/20/16: Another game. Still great, but what's taken this down a slight notch now is the 'sandbox' part of the game is actually a huge misleader. There are specific paths you have to take at the start or you are right out of the game. Still extremely fun.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 3
It's official, Splotter has laid an egg.

As I saw this title become Splotter's deepest foray into the mainstream of the hobby, I became suspicious.  Indeed, of the four of their titles I have played, this is clearly the weakest.  Not a bad game, per se, but it features substantial downtime/fiddliness (particularly during endgame) and runaway leader syndrome.

The genius of Splotter has been the subtlety with which they introduce player interaction.  The typical euro player - possessing all the social skills of Frankenstein - struggles to deal with that interaction.  He feels such an urge to react to anyone who frustrates his carefully prepared path to victory that, rather than shift to an alternative path, he seeks vengeance.

As a result, the multi-player solitaire titles multiply.  Who can build the most efficient engine to rack up VPs?

Splotter's other titles have overcome this with increased fluidity of play, i.e. paths to victory that are less clear and more easily masked.  FCM, however, is anything but fluid.  Innumerable posts on BGG attest to importance of the first hire (if not the first restaurant placement).

Given the lack of fluidity, the player attaining the early lead can be stopped only by coordinated action.  

I'll play it again, but only to appease my game group.

RATING: 10
This game is excellent. Be warned, you may transform a gaming buddy into an enemy!

There are so many different strategies that you can follow, and they are all dependent on what your opponents are trying to do. If you plan ahead, you can really screw up your opponents plans. This game can be infuriating, in a good way.

My only real complaint is that there is no in-box storage for all the pieces, no trays or bags. And after adding myown Ziploc baggies for all the pieces, the box doesn't close. For a game this expensive (I paid over $100 on Amazon, because it's out of print), I would expect better storage.

That said, the game is totally worth it, and is a new favorite in my group.

RATING: 8
I really enjoyed the deck-building aspect of hiring and training employees to fit into a corporate structure, and I'm a sucker for economic games, so I enjoyed that as well. There is also a TON of player interaction in this one: competing on prices to lure customers, launching marketing campaigns to influence demand, etc. It's a great aspect of the game, as you're always responding to what the other players are doing, and trying to carve out business for yourself.

My only main gripe is that Splotter is always pretty bad at physical component choices that make the games way harder to play than they need to be; the players not only have to learn a challenging game, but they also have to overcome unnecessarily poor component/usability choices. I'm not talking about art quality here, as that's totally subjective, but rather decisions that affect a player's ability to be able to be able to easily find and interpret important game information.

In this case, the milestone cards are so important to be able to see and read, but they provided no way for players to easily do this, even though it's easily solvable, and solvable in a way that makes the entire deck of milestone cards unnecessary, saving on table space. Speaking of which, the game takes a ton of table space, which was also partially solvable in a way that also solves the milestone card problem (I've posted an image showing this solution).

Most Splotter games I've played have this sort of issue, where the games are great, but they don't think about usability, taking away from the game experience. I like some of their games so much that I fix them with homemade modifications (I also made numerous graphical changes to my copy of Antiquity that makes it easier/faster to play by just those component mods), but most people won't want to jump through those hoops. It's a shame since this is all easily avoidable if they'd take input from someone with an eye for usability. Their playtesting shouldn't just be about game play, but also ease of play from a component perspective.

Anyways, I'm ready to play more of it to explore the game, as it's a lot to take in on the first play, as expected!

RATING: 9
Wow. The number of options available is just on the edge of complexity for me, but the theme makes it so easy to understand and digest how to play. It is very intuitive. The menu player aid is inspired and easy to follow.  

The milestones force players to either react to other hires to not fall behind on a certain path, or specialize in something the other players are not doing. It is definitely true that not getting your share of quality milestones early on is going to be tough to recover from (or from the initial placement). I think this needs to be explained to new players, but I just don't see that as much of a problem. 

I also love the tension between a short and long game, and the type of organization that benefits. I love the interplay between the organizational tech tree and the cutthroat competition on the board. 

It is a savage battle to market stuff that people can't yet produce, to work to undercut them on price, or build a new restaurant right in their territory, or new houses near your own. There are so many options to pursue, and it is all in reaction to what everyone else is doing. 

And I just love the theme and feel of it. Great game.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
:tobacco: :( :) :D :D :D Sometimes it takes someone standing outside the echo chamber to really grasp something that whooshed over your head. In my case, it was my wife, who listened to me describe Food Chain Magnate for the better part of a minute before saying "Oh, you mean 'Food Chain' like a chain restaurant, not like carnivores eating herbivores!"

I don't know if the double entendre was intentional but it's remarkably apropos; Food Chain Magnate is a rough and tumble survival of the fittest game, at times fully capable of making Dominant Species (which rather more explicity embraces that theme) look like the got-damned Ungame.

It's not wrong to point to the various progenitors of this game: 18xx's purely deterministic nature, closed economy and finite bank, Indonesia's vertiginous feeling of solid engines upended in a single stroke, a dash of pick-up-and-deliver (minor twist: the customers come to you), a bit of deckbuilding (without the hand-to-hand randomness), and a wonderful "restructuring" mechanic that functions both as simultaneous action selection AND tableau building all at once. Other stuff I'm probably forgetting off hand. Le Age of the Race For The Indonesian Galaxy's Dominion and Robber Barons.

In many cases, a game with this many ideas clanging around in it would end up being a hot mess, or at least the dreaded Less Than The Sum Of Its Parts. Not so here. The surprising and delighting thing about FCM (given its popularity among the economic-game crowd -- gotta be honest, we're nerds even by nerd standards) is that it is -- kind of accessible? Not that intimidating to learn? Attractive and fun? Even kind of silly?

I'm as surprised as you.

Now none of this to say that it's easy to pick it up and start playing well. Screw that noise. You have a starting decision to make in turn one. You can easily screw it up and spend the next two to three hours with the game metaphorically sitting on your chest saying "STOP HITTING YOURSELF! STOP HITTING YOURSELF!" And just because you managed to not blunder on turn one, don't assume that turns two and three are going to be a church sangin' with the supper on the grounds. And by the time the various players' various plans begin to truly come into contact with each other, that sweet little engine you built off in the corner, hoping no one would notice? Yeah, someone notices. And your engine shifts directly from fifth to second gear with all of the ease and subtlety you'd expect from an engine shifting directly from fifth to second gear, and you're forced into a desperate scrabble to adapt your entire business model while simultaneously, you know, making money selling hamburgers, which two goals can prove to be durned tricky to pull off in the same turn.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 9
First Impression:
Wow.  I tried my hardest to approach this title with a clear head, but couldn't help but be charmed by the lovely graphics and clever design.  Given their track record, I'm content to error on the side of optimism in an initial rating.  There is so much to like here.  I'm a little worried about the Milestones--that is, they seem unnecessary in an otherwise clean design.  Surely staffing locks players into their strategic paths with enough of a hold to make tactical pivots difficult.  But, again, given that it's a Splotter, I'm inclined to trust the added chrome.  In a very strong year, this could be my favorite title so far. 

Also: I suspect that this title will also reach a lot of new players-especially considering the nice artwork and fun theme.  But, make no mistake, this game is a bloodbath. My favorite move of hte night was aggressively using marketers to invade my opponents base with products he couldn't give them.  It locked up his profits for turns while he was forced to lay off a third of his staff until he could build another restaurant.  

Update: 
My fears about synergistic milestones were overstated.  They work just fine and give the first few turns some nice strategic consequences that extend beyond the tactical snipes of most other business game.  That said, they aren't prescriptive either.  Tactical flexibility is just another organizational asset that can be developed or ignored.  

Update:
A couple plays later the game's connection with TGZ is becoming much sharper. They are very similar games, though after different things. Ultimately, I think the cool things about TGZ resolve into a rather neat auction game. FCM isn't like that. It's a drafting game where the desired object is only gradually manifested through the play of others. I often felt like I was building a car while not knowing exactly what course it would have to run. The Milestones provide some nice strategic pressure, and, of course, in my own car building I was determining the course as well. The balance between overarching strategy and tactical maneuver is fabulous (especially because tactical pivoting can be drafted as well). Couple that drafting game with a killer spatial element, and I think Splotter may have its best game yet.

[Apologies for the redundancy of these remarks, I'm just hosing impressions for a possible future review]

RATING: 9
Excellent and rather unique forward planning game with a solid theme. There are several clever bits that fit together: the milestones that create the strategic asymmetry but also a strong incentive to follow the other players,  the discount + distance demand capture competition, and the limited snowballing effect, since money today and money later is the same and your engine development is around command slots and recruitment and training speed. 

I found the game a joy to play, and a bit of a brain burner. One needs to be mindful, that you might lost the game in the first 15' and then have to endure 2-3 further enjoyable but non-competitive hours. That's the Splotter philosophy, so I'm ok with that.

Overall, super effort and a strong place in the permanent collection.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
FCM is a game that almost feels like it is played in two halves. The first portion of the game is all about angling for the special game length Milestone cards that will craft your strategy, and putting your corporate hierarchy structure into place. The second portion involves actually getting things done on the board by stimulating consumer demands, collecting / producing the items needed to satiate those demands, and out-angling your opposition.

As is standard for Splotter, there is a fair bit of heavy lifting in the logistics portion of the game, but this really isn't as heavy on that as many of their titles. The biggest aspects seems to be the ever nasty positional play (last early is good to follow the leader and not miss Milestones; first is good later for selling stuff, most of the time at least) and the difficulty involved in balancing production, with demand stimulation, and maximizing price.

For a game with so many interlocking mechanics, FCM feels like it is cut from whole cloth. The theme, though ridiculous in some ways, fits the game to a T and makes it easier to understand what is going on. Marketers make people want stuff, the food producers and drivers pick up that food, your staff can help set prices: lowering them in competition heavy markets, or raising them dramatically if a monopoly exists. The intricacy is in the timing, and what starts out simple ends up being considerably more complex by the end as turns get longer and the decision trees get broader. You can play a lean company that just sells a few things to the right people at the right price, or you can play for a longer game with a huge structure and massive production capabilities. There are zero random factors involved in the game itself after the initial set-up and turn order draw.

Length-wise, this seems better with less, I wouldn't want to play with more than 4, as the "each player takes their turn" system means that each player adds about an equal amount of time to the game. You really want at least a few people at the table to really get the competition flowing properly, but too many and it bogs down, especially if the corporate structures get too big.

Also, as is usual for Splotter, but perhaps even moreso here than usual, a mistake early can really put you down in this title. Every turn involves planning / making moves that will affect the next one, and because of the extreme power of the Milestone effects getting behind very far early will put you out of the game in a hurry. For games of this sort, I think of this as a benefit, but many people don't like games that lack any sort of catch-up, so be aware.

I quite like it though, and it has the benefit of being pretty fast playing once you get into it. Repeat play seems like it could get a 3 player game down to 2-2.5 hrs. tops, which is great for a game of this heft.

Aside: the Milestones really are the heart of what makes this game fun. Unless you are graduating to this from something like Ticket to Ride, just play the full game from the start; otherwise you'll be missing out on too much of the game.

RATING: 5
I can see why people like this game, but it didn't strike me as the second coming of gaming, which is what some of the early reports have sounded like. First off, I don't like tech trees in games, but I know some people do, so that's just me. The core system works, and there are definitely some interesting things there, but I really really don't like the milestone system, which is core to the game (or at least I expect fans of the game to claim so). Rather than having goals with rewards for having achieved something that took a lot of effort, like in many games, there are many rewards for completing things that are rather easy, but give permanent bonuses. I really disliked this. There's a quick gold rush for the cards in the first few turns, and if you screw that up, it's game over, 4 hours later. It's quite plausible that if I played without the milestones cards for my first game (as recommended in the rules, in fact), I would have enjoyed it more. I would also probably have preferred playing with fewer players, and playing only the first half of the game. Then layering in both the complexity, length, and more players. But I suspect that I would never like the milestones cards, particularly because it requires you to pay attention to all of the specific exceptions to the rules that each player has (if you want to actually know what players can do and not play multiplayer solitaire), and it's a lot to try to keep track of. Never mind trying to keep track of what cards each player has. No one expects to keep track of all player cards in a game like Dominion, but in a zero-randomness 4 hour game, those who can and do keep track of such things are at a distinct advantage. Those who can't or dislike the effort of doing so are at a disadvantage. I personally prefer  games where I can easily observe all game state, both common state and player state.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
Amazing game. This is the first Splotter title I've played, and it is fantastic. Initially I thought this might be an 8 or 9, but after 7 plays and discovering just how many completely different strategies were viable approaches to victory, this game easily jumped to a 10.

That said, the game is certainly as brutal and unforgiving as reviews generally indicate. I've had more than one loss whose cause I can trace directly to a single mistake on my part. In some games this would be frustrating, but for some reason in Food Chain Magnate I find even these losses satisfying. I could see that my strategy had the potential for success had I but executed it better.

There is also immense satisfaction in correctly predicting an opponent's move one turn ahead of time and then hard countering it.

I'll be keeping my eyes open for other Splotter games to try out, because if Food Chain Magnate is any indication, their designs live up to all the praise.

RATING: 7
STATUS: Played

Designer: Jeroen Doumen, Joris Wiersinga 

Publisher: Splotter Spellen

Price: 

Player Count: 3-4 (best)

Notes:

Food Chain Magnate gives you everything you need at the start of the game. It is up to you how you use them. Elegant, but simple rules. BUT don't let that fool you, it is a ruthless, cutthroat game that will leave you bleeding, even more than a men on a map style game. You have to pay attention to what your fellow players are doing during their turns. You need to pay close attention and see how you can manipulate their turns to your advantage - generally by following their actions or player order. There is not candy and unicorns in business. Bottom line is, "You will buy my lemonade, you will like it, even if you don't want it!" The only downside for me is that the theme does not appeal to me, and the components, they are drab - this is mainly related to the game board tiles, they feel like a prototype. Visually dull.

RATING: 8
The game's choices are somewhat overwhelming on a first play, and I suspect one's first few games will be dedicated mostly to coming to grips with basic strategy paths, discovering what's efficient and what's not.

For me, much of the satisfaction  was in having plans come together or in successfully throwing a wrench into someone else's plan, either of which ought to be possible while battling for 3rd place rather than 1st.

I doubt I'll ever own a copy: it really wants an experienced group (or at least one that's past the "overwhelming options" stage), and I don't think it'd hit the table often enough to create one.

RATING: 9
I think FCM is quite possibly a 10, but I'm reserving judgement until after a few more plays.

This is not a game for players having disparate degrees of experience with the game.  Give newcomers a game or two of sandbox play, before pitting them against others in serious play.

One of the beauties of this game (and there are several) is how one can look at all of the employee types, resource types, and milestones, and completely overlook how that mundane-looking little board can drive the game as much as anything else (if not more).  After just one or two plays, the board looks completely different to you than it did before, as the ramifications sink in.

A thoroughly engrossing game.

RATING: 9
[24 Feb 2017] - 9.5 (5 plays)
I love this game. Right amount of player interaction, a good amount of decisions to explore and just enough rules to make decisions in the game meaningful. There are a number of tactics out of the gate that can be taken with hiring employees and enough variation in the maps to give you different things to explore. [14 Sep 2017] - 9 (10 plays)
I still enjoy the game but it's brutal. The last game I played I made some rookie mistakes in the first two rounds and I knew that I lost by the time we flipped the reserve cards. Towards the end of the game I was picking up steam but there was no way to undo my mistakes in time. Obtaining milestones a round before others and matching their milestones is critically important. You can't get a good engine going without those abilities. You also need to know before the game what you want to pursue so you pick the right reserve amount. The rating drop here was for the game being too brutal to introduce to casual players. It seems mean to teach them, beat up on them and then never have them play the game again. Works well for players who want to make repeated plays to explore the game to it's fullest.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9.4
[March 2017 - 9.4]
Moving up the rankings for me as one of those games that hit a lot of sweetspots and does so really well, especially with the interaction and theme coming in strong. And while it might seem minor, I really like how everyone sets their structure at the same time and then has to play through it on that turn. This cuts down on the AP, but still allows for some in-game flexibility on how you carry out those actions, eg. who do I train and hire, or what and where do I advertise.

[July 2016 - 9.0]
It's quickly becoming one of my favorite games, but does have some drawbacks.

First, the positives...the strategy and replayability in the game seem pretty endless. Right off the bat it's about how the board gets laid out to determine what strategies might do better than others, especially in advertising.

I love how it combines deckbuilding, area control and engine building with no luck involved. It's like Dominion, but you chose your hand to play each time from your entire deck.And while that may seem powerful, it really takes some time to get your engine going. The area control on the board can shift turn-to-turn and it's really dependent on the advertising -- that's the turf battle. Sometimes, it might seem like a solitaire game, but it really is highly interactive.

The negatives... I love the theme and design on the cards, but the art on the board tiles is lackluster and I think the tiles themselves should have been larger in size.

The game is also pretty harsh if you make one or two mistakes, which can put you out of the game pretty fast, which isn't much fun for a longer game.For that reason, players should be all around same level.

The dinnertime counting up of the distance, discounts and other modifiers can be a little fiddly, but once you get the hang of it, it gets easier.

All in all a great game and fun to experiment with.

RATING: 8
Few small rules errors on first play, this is an eight after a few plays, but may go up with additional plays. 

Heavy, but not that complex. Brutal and very unforgiving of new players. If it's your first play, I'd recommend rotating the board tiles so there aren't too many cull de sacs; and ask whoever who has played before to guide you through placing your restaurant, as if you get this very wrong there's a good chance you will lose the game. And also don't gloss over the milestones in the rules explanation as you'll want to scoop a bunch of these as quickly as possible. 

There is a high price point on this game for what you get, though the components are nice -  in fact I pretty sure you could break the components into two halves if you meant to only ever play with two/three and give them to a friend if people do that) as there is a super abundance of cards. The milestone cards should have been smaller cards, or cardboard tiles to save on table space. I probably wouldn't want to ever play with five - four plays long enough, three is probably the sweet spot.

I love the art, the map tiles are spartan, the box is nice and compact - which will be a bit of a squeeze to put the cards back in if you decide to sleeve them.

Also the variable play time based on reserve cards can be bypassed by doubling up the starting money (at 50/75 per person) at the beginning and playing until the bank is broken once, that will fix your play time at something around the 2 hour mark dependent on player count. As I don't really like the idea of sitting down to a game that may take 2 hours, may take 4, especially on a school night. 

This is a provisional 8, though I have the feeling that this is a game of Powergrid/ Brass like quality with a theme (and I am actually from the Northwest of England so get extra giggle mileage from Brass) that's more accessible, simpler than brass, equivalent in complexity to Powergrid but less math homework like, more intuitive and (in my opinion) fun. Though I'll check the fun factor rating on replays. Also it plays excellently with two, which is a massive plus.

Regarding table space, with a print out of the milestone cards on the back of the menus (later editions only) you can stack the cards and rummage for them; same with the employees, you can stack them by colour rather than type and you have shrunk the space needed with only a little additional fiddle.

Addendum: Few recent plays have confirmed my initial rating, Its great, though I don't know if I can take it's rating higher as with new people this game can grossly alienate, making a second play unlikely. Basically it's simple but opaque and things tend to escalate quickly, leaving you feeling a little bit dumb. You can fail at numerous things often at the same time. Though I don't care, it's still my favourite economic game and after several plays the surface is still unscratched.

RATING: N/A

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
FCM is undoubtedly an amazing game. When you play it, it is breathtaking as to how much you have to think about at any one time. 

You are building an organisational business structure and a bunch of businesses and advertising campaigns on the board - the type of employee you employ determines the actions which are available to you. As usual in a good Euro, you want to do all of the actions and hence employ all of the types of employee, yet you can’t. Decisions decisions. Delicious decisions. 

And this decision space is huge. It demands a long-term strategy accompanied by short-term tactical timing decisions. Yes, it is all about timing. Miss that optimal timing and you may get cast adrift. 

And so this game ticks so many boxes that you can come to a conclusion that it could possibly be the perfect game.

So what’s not to love? I do like this game and I respect it enormously. It is the cleverest and deepest game I have possible ever played. It has a perfect balance of strategy and tactics, with no random elements at all. Yet at the moment it is a feeling of awesome respect rather than genuine love. I can’t yet love the game, like I love many other Euros. Why?

1.	The game is brutal. Fall behind when trying a strategy which doesn’t quite fly, or you slightly miss the precise timing of a round in the game, and you are out of contention. For a game which lasts many hours this could be soul-destroying.

2.	The decisions are fairly overwhelming at times and for someone like myself, without the super-intellect to work out the implications of the options, it can feel frustrating.

3.	The game models the complex business environment so successfully that sometimes I feel like I am at work. Working in the Marketing field as I do further promotes this.

4.	It is fundamentally an economic business game. These games often feel a little emotionally cold to me, even when the gameplay is as good as it is here.

5.	It is a long game. 45 minutes per player is optimistic. There is a lot to do on your turn and the downtime could be considerable, although there is always much to think about as the others take their turns.


If I compare it to other Essen 15 releases then I enjoy playing Mombasa and Ponzi Scheme more, the former for its simpler decision space, and the latter for its simpler interactive economic mechanic.

I am looking forward to more games of this masterpiece and will enjoy exploring it over the coming months. Will I ever grow to love it? Time will tell.

RATING: 8

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Sein Name trifft den harten Kern des Spiels: FOOD CHAIN MAGNATE ist in jeder Hinsicht ein anspruchsvolles Restaurant kapitalistischer Planwirtschaft. Ein sehr erwachsenes Spiel. Zum einen, weil es ein ökonomisches Profitstreben und Marktdenken einfordert, das keine Fehler verzeiht und einen knallharten, aggressiven und reaktiven Wettbewerb forciert. Konsumkampf. Zum anderen, weil FCM jedes kindlich-magische Element vermissen lässt. Hier zählt das schonungslose Business. Der Aufbau der eigenen Personalstruktur zum Abverkauf von Fast Food ist Deckbau Premium, der dem organisierten, mathematischen Verstand desto mehr entgegenkommt, je länger die Spielzeit durch eine prall gefüllte Bank bestimmt wird. Auf einen Schlag lässt sich zum Schluss der ganze Fast Food Markt exploitationmässig abschöpfen und in einem erbarmungslosen Streich eine Partie gewinnen. Vorteil Vollprofi im bedingungslosen Kapitalismus. Der Style changiert zwischen genialem 50er Jahre Wirtschaftswunder-Look und der funktionalen Nüchternheit eines Knochens, was im Mix das Spielgefühl gut widerspiegelt. FCM ist von erschreckender Autorenprofessionalität und einer thematischen wie strukturellen Brillanz, die keine Wünsche offen lässt. Splotter bleibt seinem gehobenen Ruf gnadenlos treu. FCM kann so brutal sein, dass einem der Mund offen stehen bleibt, der mit einem kalten Cheeseburger gestopft wird. A McDonalds Boardgame Experience.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

These kinds of games are interesting because you can think that you know what you're doing even if you don't. (I don't)

RATING: 9
Fascinating  & complex economic euro about running a food chain franchise.   Game is played by doing the following each turn  -  setting up an organization,  hiring new workers, producing product, planning new marketing campaigns,  building new housing development, opening new restaurants, selling product and then paying salaries and lastly creating demand for product the next turn.  

It definitely captures the feel of trying to out-think what your opponents are going to do next that is the reality of running a business. The fact that you create demand for product to be fulfilled the following turn as well as waiting a round to play the new workers you just hired requires careful planning and reading of your opponents plans.  Very punishing of mistakes.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

It is the type of game where you'll be thinking about it for days or weeks after the game is over. I already have fun stories about the first time we played it. Literally cannot wait to play this again and again. After just one play, it is my favorite game.

RATING: 5
I was excited to try FCM, as it is the first Splotter game I've played. Will it live up to the hype and the luxury cost? Some pluses: It has a nice theme, great wooden components, and overall is solidly designed. It's the solid design of a heavy economic game such as this which I think gets people who like this style of game so excited. For me, the game was very boring. The first few rounds really grated on me as I discovered how to play optimally.  A few blundered moves at the start really made me feel like I was set back for the rest of the game (fortunately, this wasn't the case so much in this session).  Eventually I grasped the basics of placement and then figured out the marketing and selling mechanics (which can be quite obtuse at first but work okay once you've got the rhythm down pat). But the whole tech-tree of employment was overbearing and the card-play lumbered along. The game felt like it crawled at points.  When players got nasty with each other it felt more annoying than  deliciously frustrating. The constant mathy-ness of figuring out whose restaurants attracted which houses was a plodding experience. And when you get down to it, I found the game just plain ugly. To summarize, I don't think this game is broken at all- it works extremely well for many people. My rating is based on personal enjoyment and also how messy and clumsy the game feels at points. For me, Food Chain Magnate overshoots my definition of "fun".

RATING: 6
First Impression (3.21.17)
I had a knee-jerk distaste for the theme and art in FCM. But it being a Splotter and at the top of so many people's list, I decided to acquiesce to Chris's desire to play it.

General Thoughts: 
-Overall, I'm not sold on as taken with it as so many are but I will try it again.
-The component quality is really good, much better than I expected. I kind of came around to the art but I still find the theme really off-putting. 
-The rule set isn't terribly complicated but I need to read the rules to better understand some things about distance, marketing, and a few other edge cases. 
The player interaction is really mean, which is great, but it can also see it making for games where a couple good turns midway or later in the game could put someone so far ahead that it forces you to just call it.

Out-standing Negatives:
-Adding the reserve money for the "2nd" round at the beginning of the game seems really off to me in that it's supposed to have strategic value but, as everyone starts off the same, it really doesn't. I'd prefer to just say at the beginning this will be a short, medium, or long game, or figure out some other way to add reserve money.
-Downtime during the 9-5 phase gets exponentially longer. The game really starts to drag. 
-Although the planning and decisions are difficult, I don't find their is much in the way of tense moments. It's like, "Oh, you stole that soda and pizza from me. Okay." I guess there's a bit of it in the poker-style bluffing of "what am I going to play this turn" but the way people reveal their employees for the round doesn't let players experience tense moments. 
-Starting with no money and the ability to hire just one person makes the game a very slow burn. This added to the feeling that there weren't really tense moments. 
-The training tree, with the majority of cards only being able to be trained via Managers gets old and further inhibits the speed of the game.

RATING: 8.5
Excellent game without luck and very high replayability.
I love games in which I don't know what's gonna happen from one game to the next and Food Chain Magnate is one of them.  Other games have high replayability because of how the cards come out or another random element.  This one amaze me because the only thing random is the initial setup of the board.
I love how you recruit and train employee in this game.  There is so many possibilities!  I want all the employee but I can't have them all!
I also love that the players need to make marketing otherwise there is no demand.  You can really mess other player's plan by marketing something they don't produce!
The 18 milestones for being the first to do certain things that gives a one time bonus or a special power for the rest of the game also change the strategy and help make each game different.
The only negative about the game is that there seems to have a runaway leader problem.  In most of the games I've played, the winner had at least 200$ more than the second place.  Because there is no luck, a player that make an early mistake will have no chance to win and will have a tough time for the next 2 hour.
I love the game nonetheless and want to play again after each game!

RATING: 10
Unbelievable game. Brutal... merciless about mistakes, but so much fun. Very interesting decisions and lots of player interaction. Absolutely worth the price. 

It will take a couple plays for new players to conceptualize how to handle their first few turns to get an engine going and how to counter different player moves. However, I wouldn't say the game is "heavy" in the sense of requiring knowledge of a ton of finicky rules, and the amount of game knowledge about strategy a player needs is much less than something like say Twilight Struggle. I taught this to my family who have been confused by games like Pandemic Legacy (I'm pretty sure I've had to explain how the Researcher works about 20 times at this point), and they were pretty up-to-speed after one play. Generally simple and intuitive rules.

Milestones are HUGE in this game, do not sleep on those. They grant huge benefits, especially in early game where missing out on the right milestones can really hurt your plans for a while. The importance of milestones and the significance of management trainees as the gatekeepers before all of the most powerful staff are the two biggest things I recommend teaching.

Depending on how you read it, the lack of women in management positions could be considered satire of 1950s culture or actual misogyny. My group chose to read it as the former, but this may be a sticking point for some.

Who will like it:
Strategy fans who like rich games filled with interesting decisions. Players who like player interaction and don't feel personally slighted by other player decisions that can ruin their own plans. People who like to strategize about contingencies, line up a turn that accomplishes several goals, or sabotage their opponents. People who think engaging in a burger arms race after a radio advertising campaign is hilarious. People who are willing to shell out more than average for a board game.

Who should skip it:
People who can't deal with having their plans dashed. Table flippers. People who don't like to be attentive to the turns of other players. People who aren't looking to pay about double what the average board game costs. People who aren't looking to play about a 3 hour game (though interestingly my games were closer to 2 hours after the initial game, so take some of the longer estimates with a grain of salt. Time spent thinking about a turn definitely dominates amount of time resolving a turn here).

RATING: 9

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Food Chain Magnate is pretty darn cool: it's a competitive fast food economy in the era of booming fast food. It's quirky, it's vicious, and surprisingly pretty straight forward for a game of its weight.

As the newest Splotter on the block, this game offers more of what they boutique publisher is known for heavier Euro fare with a focus on economics, logistics, timing, and positional play -- all of which pulled together by a sensible theme. FCM does not buck this trend but rather pivots on it a bit. It's closest relative, the Great Zimbabwe, has a closed economy, but not here! Money is earned, money is spent in ways that are open (who knows where that money goes, its the American dream/nightmare!) -- in turn, you are *almost* wholly responsible for your revenue... if it weren't for everyone around you undercutting your costs, advertising products you can't deliver on, or even hiring key staff before you can! The small-ish map means that every game has players coming into inevitable competitive and collusive relationships: this is where the fun occurs! Throw in a bit of typical Splotter turn-order shenanigans and it is tense. As an economic game, it provides milestones (i.e., powers) as an incentive to not treat the structure strictly as a snowball: this encourages many different approaches (e.g., early food spoiling means you get a freezer for keeping up to 10 food items between turns - great if demand surpasses supply!).

While this isn't the most mechanically accessible Splotter, the congruence between theme and mechanics create a real sense of narrative that many players may already be familiar with. Splotter definitely has a hit on their hands with this one.

RATING: 10
Played one introductory game and one full game. Both 3 players. From reading the rules it was clear that this was an extremely elegant game in terms of its design.

The mechanics are very thematic and are very easy to grasp. Creating your corporate structure each turn is so elegant and engaging that it just does it for me. 

The artwork is phenomenal. Some people are complaining about the design of the board but I think it's completely adecuate. Also, best player aid ever!!

Grasping the rules is easy although mastering them is quite hard. Very unforgiving if you do some mistakes but when it works its that much more rewarding.

Overall a great experience. Only downfalls I see is that it's a game that you can only play with people that have played before unless you are learning it at the same time. Also for your first plays read some strategy guides if you don't want to completely screw yourself in the first turns.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
I really love this game.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9.6
I love how the rules are relatively  simple to grasp but the play can be very complex/deep. The initial board setup is random, but the cards available for purchase is the same. Your strategy very much will depend on what other people decide to do. If you play in your own corner of the board and are oblivious of other people's actions/plans, they could very well muscle in to your area and steal your customers.

The rules recommend the first game to be without salaries, milestones, and breaking bank only once. An intro game seems to be about 8-10 rounds, which is 60-90 minutes. I've started mentioning this when teaching because the end comes much sooner than people expect. Players tend to spend a lot of time hiring people to hire people to hire/train/train/etc, and by the time they are ready to start selling things, the game is over.

I've finally gotten to play the full game and I LOVE LOVE LOVE it. Can't get enough. I find myself jotting down notes on what I project I will do current turn (because I don't want to forget when it comes to my turn) and for planning what I might do or try to do in future rounds. These notes constantly are shifting based on what other players do.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

My initial opinion is that the play who gets best set of milestones in the first hour will win the game in 2-4 hours time.

RATING: 4

You may think that's the game you want, but there's at least the potential for a severe runaway leader problem and with no catch up mechanism you can easily find yourself chugging along in last place while opportunities multiply for the leaders. This is a very tough game to rate, it's a heavy thinker and with players of equal skill this could be a classic, but for most groups I'm afraid it's simply broken.

RATING: 7
Comments:
I have a few concerns about it. For one, the opening hires seem to be pretty heavily scripted, with quite a few options seeming to be bad or at best highly conditional. Second, new players are going to get DEMOLISHED unless they are either incredibly talented, or read a lot beforehand. It takes a session or two to really grok the game, and it makes no secret of the fact that new players are playing badly. They will lose, it will not be fun, and it will be a slow death.

All that being said, the game is wonderfully intense. From start to finish you need to be watching and reacting to everyone else. If you don't keep an eye on your vulnerabilities, they will be exploited mercilessly. If you can find a table of players that enjoy that sort of a dynamic, this is a great fit. Fatigue has been a real issue in my games so far, as one mistake can end things for you. It gets tiring to be constantly trying to telescope the game out multiple turns in the future to figure out what you need to build toward.

Fun is the wrong word, but it's close. The best way I can phrase it is that this game is fulfilling. If you win, YOU won. If you lost, YOU lost. That sort of direct control over your fate is both exhilarating and terrifying.

Rating it a 7 for now, but I could see it rising with more play.

Sleeved in Fantasy Flight Clears.

Alfre
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
For how incredibly mentally-demanding this game is, the rules are fairly light! Probably most brilliant is how this game all but negates "optimal strategies" given its huge emphasis on player interaction (and reaction!) with its modular setup.

Artwork is wonderful. Minimal, perhaps, but the graphic design is brilliantly cheeky ("100% Dice Free!"). The player aids shaped as diner menus was the kicker.

Not the explosion of tiny square pieces like Indonesia / Antiquity / Roads & Boats. Plenty of wooden food tokens and SO MANY CARDS. The table-space needed for the cards is one of the most unreasonably large I've seen. At least the rest of the game is fairly tidy!

Unreasonable card space aside; the game is a lot less "fiddly" than, well, probably any Splotter I've played. Maybe Greed Inc. was more ergonomic but there's so much more in Food Chain Magnate. A problem with Splotter games in general is the amount players tend to fiddle with pieces is more than average; FCM seems to be very minimal in this aspect (except for laying out the humongous card space).

Every mechanic in this game is ingenious. Reading the rules for the first time, every part of the game seemed to do something clever for the game. For example, while this is a game that ends when the bank runs of money (player with most wins), the bank has to run out *twice* because of secret "reserve" cards. At the start of the game each player is given a set of these cards, each has a different amount of money. After picking starting positions on the main board, each player secretly chooses one of their reserve cards to be a portion of the money that fills the second bank (that is, the total amount of money on players' chosen cards is the second bank). The full amount is kept hidden until the first bank empties; obfuscating the game length but still giving each player a portion of control to that length.

The game is built on twists of normally mundane mechanics, and FCM is so much better for it. The action economy is a twist on deck-building ("there's no deck, but instead..."). There are permanent-yet-limited-in-quantity benefits players can grab that is a twist on achievements. Competing in selling goods isn't just "lowest price sells" (the twist is that your consumers will by more expensive food and drink if its closer than the other chain...assuming you have all the food and drink they want!)

While longer than Great Zimbabwe, this one is quickly becoming my favorite game among Splotter's designs (otoher ones played being Roads & Boats &cetera, Antiquity, Indonesia, Greed Inc., Indonesia). Considering Zimbabwe and other titles by the designer are some of my favorite games of all time; this is high, high praise.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 9
This is one of the best games I have played in the past decade. It's an exception for me, in that the length and rules complexity* are so worth it. I love how theme-y the game is; it makes the rules seem intuitive. I also love the art and graphic design, and this game has one of the best player aids I've ever seen. 

*The rules complexity is really not that bad, but it's there. There are a lot of steps in a turn, and the game is overwhelming to new players because of all the cards. 

Half the game is an efficiency thing where you're "building an engine" to make more stuff than your opponents. That's fine, and rather brain burn-y, and you'll be best off if you can anticipate what your opponents will be doing. But the heart of the game is in the other half -- the advertising --  which leads to wonderfully intense interaction. It feels like freakin' warfare. But with burgers and pizza.

This game has teeth. First-timers will likely lose by a lot to experienced players. An early mistake can take you out of the running. I don't consider these bad things, you just need to be ready for an intense game.

RATING: 8
I loved the way you collected and managed employees with the cards, and the milestones were really cool, since you can generally make sure you get the ones you really want if you're careful but it involves watching everything your opponents do. You have to be even more careful about the board stuff. It seemed like you have to jump through a lot of hoops to get sales going and it's easy for an opponent to jump in and undercut you, which definitely fits the theme. It's also very hard to react to if you don't see it coming ahead of time; it usually takes multiple turns of no income to get back into a position where you can make money. So I can see that being really frustrating for some people, but also a big plus for others. I'm sort of on the fence about it. Generally I like a little less aggressive amounts of interaction, but here I found myself really enjoying the potentials for jumping in to someone else's market and watching for others trying to do the same to you. Definitely one I'd love to play more to see how different games can evolve and try new strategies.

Update: It's losing a bit of its luster for me. I'm really not a fan of the scripted openings, and just in general I feel like there's nothing too unique about it other than the multiple turn delay between when you choose to make an adjustment and when that comes into effect. But I feel like if I want a tight economic game of jockeying, there are a number of better options (Arkwright, the better 18xx). Also, there are some really involved procedural parts that if you want to be playing your best you'd probably spend a lot of time counting out, but that would make the game drag a lot. Still good, but there are enough blemishes for it to be out of the top tier for me.

RATING: 8
This is a fun very very deep game with great theme.  Each player is running a fast food business in a small town.  You have to make the food, stoke up household appetites with ad campaigns, and then compete to feed them with the closest and cheapest food.  Every round you choose which employees to deploy and a lot of the strategy involves which new employee types you recruit each turn and which you choose to put into play.  There is a lot of outguessing your competitors which makes for high drama.  An especially interesting twist is the milestones wherein the first players to perform a certain action (friendly ties) get a permanent reward that is often quite powerful, reinforcing the multiple paths to victory aspect of the game and powering often intense races for certain milestones.  This is a Splotter game, which is a Dutch garage outfit that is infamous for well-designed games with poor components.  In this case the components are good quality and reinforce the 50's theme quite well.

RATING: 10
