=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
2016-12-30

Initial Rating: 7.5 (December 2016)

Splotter games are always unique, even the ones I don’t like have had fresh and innovative ideas so I’m always open and excited to try more of their games. Food Chain Magnate has very good reviews and I’m happy to report I really enjoyed the game.

The game is very expensive as is usual from them, but I don’t mind much about that. For once, if I like the game then I REALLY liked it so it is well spent money, and If I don’t like it I can sell the game and recoup the money I spent in the first place.

The theme isn’t exciting for me, but it works very well with the game mechanisms, still this is a very minor issue to me.

The game components are ok (which is bad for such an expensive game), but, if you compare them with previous Splotter games, they are really good. They are improving and that’s great. The art is ok and it serves its purpose. In any case the components and art are always secondary to gameplay for me. It has great player aids. The graphic design works very well.

The setup can be a bit annoying and it requires a big table to play.

The rules aren’t complex, but there are many rules. You can explain the game under 20 minutes. Playtime is around 3 or 4 hours, still as the game is so immersive I don’t care much. I think it´s best played as a 3 player game, with more players the downtime begins to be annoying.

Food Chain Magnate offers a plethora of good and interesting choices (Which worker to recruit?, Which worker to upgrade?, When to upgrade?, Which workers should work this phase? Which product should I buy/generate?, Which advertising campaign should I play and where? Who is delivering the same products as me?, Which milestone should I grab?). You also need to analyze when the game will finish or it can upset your plans (I lost this way, I needed one more turn to win but had I realized the game was about to finish I think I could have won as well :D) and that’s great! Zero luck factor, a huge plus for me in an strategy game. To me, Food Chain Magnate has a medium-heavy weight game.

The game can be mean in the sense that your plans can be for naught and all your food can be lost, I love it :D. This can happen very easily if you don’t watch your opponents moves, so the game is highly interactive, you are always fighting for the limited demand. As is usual from Splotter, one mistake can take you out of the game (this is a positive for me).

The replayability factor of the game is high due to its modular board. It is a bit reminiscent of another great Splotter game, Indonesia.

Bottom line, Food Chain Magnate is a winner in my book and a keeper for me. I really want to play it again soon and I’m pretty sure my SO will love it as well. It can perhaps get an upgraded rating from me with more plays. It is a satisfying Euro game with very interesting decisions and no luck, just my kind of game. 

Current Rating: 7.5

RATING: 10
Phew. This game is daunting. From the almost blank box to the dry rulebook, Food Chain Magnate is an exercise in board game teaching and learning discipline. But the visual design, game design and delicious, delicious strategy all come together in what is now my favourite heavy game. Abuse your friend's expensive radio marketing to sell your cheap cheap chain pizzas, or make a mom-and-pop Lemonade stand run by volunteers. Create demand for things your opponents can't produce, and run a beer and burgers Mafia in your own corner of a tiny town. One of the best things about this game is the stories that it creates by virtue of its fantastic mechanics, and that is what will keep me coming back again and again. Like a deep-dish pizza all to yourself, FCM is oh-so-heavy but oh-so-delicious and truly satisfying.

RATING: 6
Oooh..where to begin.
The combination of missed rules and info overload at the beginning of the game caused major confusion and poorly played early turns.  With that, this is a pure asshole euro. Not "I'm gonna kill your troops" area control asshole as in Kemet or Cry Havoc, but pure sneaky "I'm gonna steal your shit" kinda euro similar to that of Dominant Species. Those who complain about the solitaire experience of Agricola or even Terra Mystica will adore the interaction Food Chain Magnate offers. 
There are several worker paths which can provide fun exploration of the game. Turn 1 storage seems a necessity, but from others' comments, it's merely a crutch for noobs. I can appreciate that.
There is no catch-up mechanic, so early mistakes simply mean you lost. That's a significant difference between FCM and Dominant Species.
We'll see if this has enough legs to warrant enough plays for the group to completely 'get it'. I doubt it will, but we'll see. If I get more plays in, I suspect this game may reach an 8 or 9. But after my one play, I believe there are plenty of other tried and true great games that deserve my attention. Back to the first sentence, this may be a case that the first play semi-spoiled my experience.
Wooh! After a second playing, I am done! I appreciate why others enjoy the interaction and constant changing game state this title offers. There's most likely a solid title here for those who enjoy their euro with heavy 'asshole' player interaction, but I prefer that mechanism in my area control games, not Euros.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
JUEGAZO!

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
Dense mechanics, but in an extremely satisfying and (mostly) elegant way.  Engaging, complex choices balancing corporate structure, marketing and food service mechanics, made more thrilling by tough inter-player competition.  The reward of setting your structure /just right/ to steal customers from your neighbor - I can't remember a gaming moment so satisfying.

Only downsides are 1) excessive setup time and tabletop needs; 2) lots of mechanics and their interplay to master (not a downside to me, but to some); 3) unforgiving to new/young players -- early bad decisions can wreck you, made worse by 4) long game times.  Last partially mitigated by player choices, but only partially.

Downsides restrict when it can make it to the table (and with whom), but the experience itself is worth it.

RATING: 8
my main complaint about this game is the ratio of parsing the game state to the decisions is pretty high.  mid/late game it is just really difficult, but completely possible, to parse exactly what is already going to happen the current and even the next turn, but also what the players going after you are likely to do.  this parsing is insanely difficult though sometimes.  so when you screw up it feels not like the other players outplayed you but you just failed to count correctly or failed to see one single move into the future.  it's like when you make a bonehead move in chess and immediately get your queen captured. RATING: 10
Not all Splotter games are great, or even good, but they are always interesting and without exception worth trying. The best games create an open play space with a few boundaries (without a lot of rules overhead) that allow the players to invent creative strategies and form their own journey through the game. Clearly these games are not for everyone, but for those who get excited by a truly blank palette of possibiities at the games' beginning, no one does it better than Splotter.

FCM is just such a game, possibly the game with the most open game play and possibilities of any of their titles (or very close to Roads & Boats in this regard). I have not scored any of their games a "10" before now, however, because there is always a little bit of awkwardness in some of the details of a Splotter game, which I don't mind but it keeps it from getting a top grade. FCM is really a smooth and streamlined realization of what they do best. Every turn was filled with "ah HA!" moments and "holy crap" realizations of what is possible. In our first game, one player made as much money as the rest of us combined, which enabled us to talk long and excitedly about what he did well and where we went wrong. There will always be great conversations born out of games of FCM and always something new to try. My top ten of all time needs to make room for this one.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Thoughts after 1st play (3ply session):

I was hesitant to pick up FCM for quite sometime do to what I saw as an over-emphasis on engine/deck-building, combo-creating card play, and the overall number of cards. I tend not to like a game with a million upfront options and lots of actions to choose from but rather prefer a small menu with a limited set of actions to hone. 

I found my first play of FCM to largely be what I initially feared. An emphasis on engine/deck building with tons of options, a growing number of action possibilities, and more semi-direct player interaction on the board. I see how everything still comes down to undermining your opponents on the board; however, the time spent on building and ramping oneself up toward board shenanigans is a little long for my l taste. Rating 7.

Thoughts after 2nd play (4ply session):

Wow that was easier and faster (we didn't play the reserves though just $400 for 4 players). I focused largely on board development and built my structure in response to how I wanted to utilize the board. I did mimic some milestones of one opponent whose restaurant was close to mine, but otherwise I focused on maximizing the board. My path was a combo of discount manager and business developer with some minor cooking/advertising. I won $260-60-50-30. Rating 8.

Thoughts after 3rd play (2ply session):

Played through the first bank with my wife tonight (just $200). It was her first time and she ended up with more employees, 15-11, and more milestones 8-7, than I but I used the business developer to build two houses, add a garden, and coupled it with a radio campaign and burger cook to sail to an easy and quick victory. My wife commented that she was actually understanding it by the end. However, when asking her about playing it again she ops for other Splotter's. Rating 7.

Thoughts after 5th play:

Well I think this is settling at a 7 for me. I admire it more than like it. I just don't enjoy building my corporate structure, the time it takes to upgrade from one thing to the next. I have the same issue with Antiquity; it takes multiple turns to implement something. I like it once it gets going but the first half of the game is just boring to me.

General Thoughts:

I think FCM, like many a Splotter is a brilliant design. This is the most interesting and interactive engine-builder I've ever played. I can see why this is so immensely popular. It combines a fresh take on a popular mechanism, deck/engine-building, with an intuitive theme, fast-food business, with goals to be achieved (milestones), and board play that has potential for nasty play.

That said I definitely prefer other Splotter's, particularly TGZ. In FCM here seems a shift in emphasis toward more longterm engine building and planning vs the more instant reactionary play and direct player interaction and interdependence of TGZ.  While there are still board shenanigans to be had, they are easier to plan for (most things are a turn away) and nobody can directly impair your engine vs TGZ where things occur that same turn and people can render your craftsman obsolete.

RATING: 6
I love a lot of things about this game, but I also dislike several things. The action selection is excellent as are the multiple paths you can take in the game. Player elimination is not in the game, but it essentially is. It is not uncommon for a player to be completely without hope of winning by the mid-game.

I have other concerns about the balance (especially the first to $100 milestone). But I hope that with more plays some of those will prove to be unfounded.

RATING: 5
Ok, I have to hand in my membership card to geekdom. The new Splotter is not for me. I fail to adore the special skill combo building, I'm even annoyed by the heavy snowball effect. I was reminded of Outpost, one of my worst gaming experiences. I have no desire at all to figure out the best starting moves, to fine-tune a long-term strategy, to read the board, to find killer moves.

The milestones offend me most. For a comparison, in [gameid=171623] every player is handed one really strong skill. In Food Chain Magnate the first player to reach a certain milestone gets awarded such a strong skill. Unless all players know what they do and which milestones are most important, it is entirely possible and probable that after the first three or four turns the game is already decided by the distribution of the special skills with a single player claiming most or all essential skills. Many players will love this. I'm turned off by the requirement to learn this. 

The seemingly open nature of the game initially is not as open as it makes believe. Classic Euro engine building is a must: recruit, train (or vice versa), followed quickly by marketing. The sandbox starts only after the initial moves when the key milestones are distributed. At that point, Food Chain Magnate already lost me. The interesting part which should follow with equally skilled players will be lost on me.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
The most interesting new game I've played this year, with a lot to recommend it - but also a few important flaws.
It feels a lot like the supply and demand game Automobile should have been. The most important innovation is letting players determine the demand themselves, which is one of those excellent ideas that only feels obvious in hindsight. This fundamental core of the game, involving managing supply and demand to try to undercut the other players, or to carve out a unique but lucrative niche for yourself, is fantastic. In particular the ability to create markets for certain specific combinations of goods is fascinating, and offers a much richer set of decisions than the "sell x, or y, or z" decisions found in similar games.
Unfortunately, instead of emphasising the fundamental interaction to fulfil demand between the players, a lot of the trappings get in the way of it. Both the milestones and the management structure serve, in different ways, to limit players' future options. This shifts decision making from questions like "Should I increase demand or production? And of what?" - which are based on interacting with other players on the shared board - to significantly less interesting questions like "What specific ordering of hires do I need to have the correct option for two turns' time?" - which are based solely on managing one's own board. In a similar way, the milestones serve to limit players' future options by making certain plays much more desirable - and so shrink the decision space for the later game.
It's difficult to see what purpose all this serves. While one can make the argument that experienced players will be fighting each other for future opportunities, why not make these opportunities clearer to begin with, so that players can get on with interacting with each other instead of with the game system?
Overall, it's a fascinating game. I keep thinking about it, and would be interested to play more given the chance. But I suspect I won't be able to get rid of the feeling that the system could have been so much more interesting in a more streamlined form.

Imperial gives you a very simple framework, and leaves all the complexity in playing the other players; FCM gives you a page-long sequence of play and three or four dozen stacks of cards, each with special powers, and with various synergies you need to figure out before your first move.

RATING: 7
Das Spielprinzip ist recht einfach. Jeder versucht seine Fast Food Kette zur erfolgreichsten zu machen, bevor die Bank pleite geht.
Alle Spieler überlegen sich am Rundenanfang, wie sie ihre Unternehmensstruktur aufbauen (Welche Karten sie ausspielen). Manche der Karten erlauben weitere Karten zu spielen, andere erlauben Burgen oder Pizza zu machen, wiederrum andere machen Werbung für Produkte, dann gibt es noch Fahrer, die Getränke besorgen können, Servierdamen und und und. Nachdem alle Spieler ihre Karten gewählt haben, sind sie der Reihe nach dran um sie auszuführen. Produkte die man produziert, kann man nur verkaufen, wenn Kunden auch das Bedürfnis dazu haben, was wiederrum duch Werbung erschaffen wird. Dabei kann eine Werbung die ich schalte die Bedürfnisse von Anwohnern wecken, diese dann aber wiederrum zum Konkurenten gehen, weil der einfach näher oder billiger ist.
Leider kam bei uns dieser kompetative Ansatz erst kurz vor der ersten sprengung der Bank und somit nicht wirklich zum tragen. Das kurze Spiel ist daher nicht zu empfehlen.
Ich würde mich aber nochmal zu einer längeren Partie überreden lassen.
Ein Spiel das ich kaufen würde, ist es allerdings nicht, da wir bei der kurzen Variante schon 1,5h gespielt haben und wir lange Spiele einfach nicht oft auf den Tisch bringen. Ausserdem gefällt mir die Optik (Grafik kann man es nicht nennen) des Brettes nicht. Die Produktionsketten zu produzieren gefällt mir aber sehr gut.
-> Es hat mich auch stark an "Dungeon Petz" erinnert, bei dem man kleine Viecher züchtet, die Bedürfnisse haben (Käfig reinigen, spielen, Fleisch/Salat, Magie...) die befriedigt werden wollen. Allerdings ist die Mechanik deutlich anders (Worker Placement).

RATING: 10
Presently my favorite game.
It is a heavy strategic game with no luck element at all and you have to carefully plan several moves ahead. On the other side, the game is very interactive, even cofrontational and you must not ignore what your opponents are doing. 
The rule book is lengthy but well written and once playing, the rules make a lot of sense and are rather intuitive. The playing aid is excellent in content and artwork and you won't have to look in the rule book anymore.
One concern are the milestone cards. They are important, but there are many of them and it is difficult to keep track of when you earn one.
Artwork is refreshingly different. 
Best played with up to 4. A 5 person game can take very long.

RATING: N/A
Players: 2–5 
Best: 3-4

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
For a game with a 4.2 game weight this was actually fairly easy to learn. Also, while presumably in the normal game you can be punished very severely for playing poorly in the beginning and your first few opening moves seems to somewhat lock you into a strategy, at least in the intro game there felt like a greater sense of direction despite the numerous card stacks compared to something like say, Agricola, where I've seen players really flounder without doing anything. My biggest issue with the game is that while there are 3 different drinks and 2 different foods, they all feel very samey and so while there are certainly different strategies you can pursue with the pseudo tech trees of training workers, the general feel of the game may feel the same each time (and as always I wish there was variable setup, as in more than just the board). However, I was quite pleased by the level of interaction: you can actually advertise for houses your opponents are serving to make their demands so exorbitant that your opponent can't meet their demands, you can snipe the customer base of opponents from them or leach off the demands they generate from advertisements, or try to monopolize a food type while selling at high prices. Also, something I didn't realize from just reading the rules is that the order of which house looks for food first can be quite significant, and you may have resources you would want to spend on a more lucrative, later house be exhausted too early. Such interactions can make turn order quite important so you can react to your opponent's actions, and can also make it hard to just plan things out by yourself. The cutthroat nature of it feels quite at home in the capitalist 1900's fast food chain theme.

I like the building up your hired staff with the pseudo tech tree of the workers. Kind of afraid there is runaway leader (while there is more upkeep for more workers, I think generally it is still better to have more), but gives an engine building feel. Also you starting with only your CEO gives you a few rounds to get a general feel of the game before things get crazy.

Obviously didn't play with the milestones, but think they would give you more reason to try different things from game to game.

I thought the artwork would bother me, but actually the card art I think fits with the style well, and the colorfulness of the wooden pieces adds some vibrance. The only think that really bothers me is how bland the board looks.

RATING: 10
Very solid Splotter Spellen game.  It may be their best release to date.  Components are very nice, especially the cards with the 50's theming.  The board is functional.  It is relatively easy to learn and the mechanics are streamlined.   Criticism of the art is a little silly.  It very much fits the 50's theme.  

I will say the that the game starts very slow.  It can seem that the it might never end especially with new players.  But the game does accelerate to the end very quickly.  It has a sandbox feel as there are so many choices.   Once a few players gets their engine going, the game accelerates to the end quickly.  

This is my number 3 game out of 555 unique games played over the past few years.  I love this game.  It seems that all paths have their strengths and weaknesses and you have to react to what other people are doing.

Having said this, it is a bear to teach (45 min to 1 hour) and it has a learning curve.  With newbies at the table, the game can result in runaway wins for one player with one player running away with it.  5 player games often go 3 hours.

RATING: 2.5
Only played it online at boardgamecore.net.  Played 2p and 3p, with all features.  Unforgiving.  That single adjective best captures the nature of it.  Feels like work and leaves you with a bad taste in your mouth.

This is a game like Chess and Go in the sense that a difference in skill determines the winner every time.  The question then becomes: (1) is it worth investing the time and energy to master it and (2) can it be played with a group of friends of unequal skill level with everyone (including the less skilled players) enjoying the match?  My personal answers to both of those questions are no.  I already have a hard time when I win a game with friends where they did not enjoy the ride, I don't think I would be able to emotionally handle or enjoy winning a 3-4 hours game of this against less skilled players and/or lose a 3-4 hours game of this against more skilled players.  I also believe that with repeated plays a series of preferred openings will be apparent (as in Chess), and knowing/memorizing those openings and sticking to them will be needed, limiting the illusion of individual freedom.  Perhaps I am just too lazy to work when it's time to play, and this game requires work and gives little room for the kind of social interaction I enjoy:  the auctions, bluffing, negotiating, social deduction, and opportunities for shock and laughter that I anjoy.  I don't usually like to have to think three/four steps ahead and plot out the different outcomes to figure the best action, I like to follow my instinct, and here it will almost guarantee that I will be inconsequential, like playing Chess against a master relying on one's instinct only.  Last -- every time I play I feel bad -- games are supposed to be fun, and this one somehow makes me feel miserable both during and at the end, and no amount of cute graphics is going to fix that.

RATING: 9.5
First impressions : 
no game surprised  so much as this title recent years. 
The first play, I didn't understand what I was doing, but I a was already blown away !
very streamlined rules ; no exceptions, a jewel of board game design.  Good theme. Original game play.
Tactical and strategic possibilities are overwhelming.
Very deep and interactive game. 
I really need more plays of this wonderful game.

update after 5 plays : 

Short description : chess 2.0  with a food taste

Long description :   
-Unique pick-up-and-deliver game
-Fantastic spatial and card puzzle with a twist.

The game is excruciatingly difficult : 
 - Every turn is an optimisation exercise in job choice, money management, food production (5 different ones!), marketing, payroll, available number of slots, jockeying for turn order position next round...  If you fail in one category, you are maybe already in trouble !
  -  It's a merciless race game ; you always have to be aware what the other player's goals are; not only to grab the milestones, but also to see if you are  behind in the race of the tob jobs.
Keeping up with the pace of the other players is important. 

The game rules are streamlined ; excellent integration of theme and mechanics. 
But make no mistake : you need at least 10 games to have a  grasp of  the spatial subtleties of the modular board, jobs and milestones. The relative importance of the jobs and milestones  is heavily influenced by the initial board position. Delicate balance between short term and strategic goals. 

The game starts very slow, but suddenly accelerates with incredible speed. You desperately try to follow the insane pace of the game ; it boils down to struggle for adaptation versus going your own way...unless you are ahead in the race. Then you can rule the waves. 

The game is very unforgiving ; one error... and you can hope to do better next game !

RATING: 3
I won't get into too much detail since it might as well be a giant review, so I'll just list my main issues:

- Horrible artwork with a drab color scheme does not warrant the high price tag

- Runaway leader problem, mainly due to a silly mechanic centred around "sell all or nothing"

- Way too much clean up and set up.  With the numerous amount of card piles, there is no tray or anything to organize them well so it takes up the entire table.  Again, this is a game priced at a premium, this should've been included.

- A game with simple mechanics that overstays its welcome.  The only difference between the beginning of the game and end of the game is higher numbers.  Instead of producing 1 burger, you produce 8.  Hooray.  Like Risk, you're doing the same thing over and over again for several hours.

- Too much downtime, especially if you're not leading.  As I've mentioned in my previous point, the longer game is just higher numbers.  You have 5 or 6 slots filled with cards?  The person before you has 12.  They will take 10 to 15 minutes to finish their turn.

- Unforgiving to the point where you can tell who can win before the bank first breaks, making the length of the game burn into your psyche.  Even after 3 hours, the rest of the table conceded (we were about 2 or 3 turns away) because it was obvious who would win.

Probably the worst game of 2017 and makes me question how this mess of a Eurogame managed to get the praise and high rating.

RATING: 7
2016 Top 100 Favorites of All-Time: #95
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Preliminary rating: 9

Comments: After 1 play with milestones, I have to say that there is A LOT going on in this game. Yes, the game is intuitive. True, the mechanisms are not overly complex. But, there's still a ton to keep track of and look out for. I found that, in order to teach this game (after reading the rule and watching a play through), I still needed to walk through the directions step-by-step rather than paraphrasing. That said, after just 1 play, I could probably explain it in half the time. The gameplay is quite interesting in that it is *extremely* slow building, which I'm guessing is supposed to simulate a slow start to a new business. In other words, for the first 3-4 rounds, you are not completing the full round because you haven't fulfilled all the stages. That's quite rare. I'm used to this in the first round, but not first several rounds. It didn't bother me though. In fact, I enjoyed that slow build-up, especially while I was learning the game. I wonder if I'll enjoy it as much in future games, or if it will feel "scripted." Either way, I'm curious to find out. Another thing that you should be aware of is that this is a LONG game. With 2 players, both playing for the first time (including setup, takedown and rules explanation), this game took about 4.5 hours. So, be mindful of that when you are considering breaking this one out (and we are not typically AP prone). I can see how the game is unforgiving, but at the same time, my wife went being ostensibly out of the game, to taking a commanding lead in one turn. So, for all it's "harshness,"  the game is quite swingy, which can be good for catch up. Last, I will say that the game has a sandbox feel. The variability in strategy seems nearly infinite, with so many career paths to follow. That's a good thing in my mind.

Do I like the game? Yes. Do I love the game? Yes. The components are awesome, with the little food tokens and cutesy pieces. It definitely evokes a 1950's theme. I really like the card backs, the thick rulebook, and most of all, the player aid. For a Splotter, this game is top-notch. Even the box is quite sturdy and a good size (Treefrog size). Most importantly, I feel like I'm running a 1950's fast food chain. A home run! I can't wait to play again.

RATING: 9
Bought from funagain games flash sale 12/12/16.

Amazing game that maintains a high level of tension with every hire and decision point.  Every action really has far reaching consequences.  This does, however, work against the game a bit, as it results in scripted openings (Recruiting Girl 1st turn to get first to 3 hires).  However, the game really does open up.  It's sandboxy a-la caverna, but your opponents' play / competition for milestones / map layout do give some direction.

The "career paths" (aka tech trees) are my favorite part of the game.  It feels somewhat like leveling up a character in an old school RPG and teching their skills up.  This constant cycle of recruiting / training / producing ensures that the game has a smooth sense of escalation that results in satisfying amounts of building.

One of the great concerns with FCM was its ability to be mean.  This usually comes from the ability of an opponent to steal business with more effective advertising or price cutting.  While it is competitive, it doesn't feel mean.  Rather, the timing of advertising gives you a 1 turn warning, allowing you to plan a counter strategy.  It's not mean on the level of something like Dominant Species, where you're tearing down an opponent.

Art on the cards is great and thematic.  However, the tile art looks like something from a prototype.  Our enjoyment of this was greatly increased after making Chris Cormier's upgraded tiles.

+ High Interaction
+ Lots of tension
+ Career paths offer variety and satisfying sense of building
+ Highly thematic mechanics
o Highly competitive, but not destructively mean
o Very sandboxy, but the board does offer some direction
- Scripted openings
- Tile art

RATING: 8.8
[b]2016-01-09 8.8[/b] After 2x Intro 2x Full Plays:
Great strategy game where every. single. move. matters. In the proper full game, the accomplishment of initial milestones define your general strategy for that particular game, but player still has to pay close attention to the board and seize opportunity to sell. Unlike typical engine-building games, if your build up is not quick enough, other players might break the bank and force a game end. Each game feels different and scales well across different player count as the board size changes. The level of "cut-throat-ness" is the group's choice. One could play a mean game and screw with others' target households without direct benefit to oneself. A good tug of war in the closed economy, this game would have got an even higher rating if it doesn't reward the experienced player that much.

RATING: 5
Tenuous - only keeping due to it being a "Splotter"

Good but eh, something is irksome in a bad way compared to my favourite, Zimbabwe. 

It's far less abstracted and more sprawling than Zimbabwe (the closest parallel that I know of). Clearly  there are aspects shared by both in terms of logistics and utilising your few but powerful special powers. Removed is the auction and the "sloshing" of money between players (this is much closer to a snowball-type game, but straddles the line as your engine is never anything more than a hard fought positional advantage), and the theme makes this game instantly much easier to grasp (after all, market, sell, earn money isn't a foreign concept, while gift-giving in Zimbabwe kind of is).

What makes this game for me is the spatial aspect - it doesn't feel like an afterthought like it does in Keyflower, but really is central to the game and that's how it avoids becoming a strict snowball efficiency-race. So you hire, you market, you train, you fire, you produce and sell and do your best to maintain an advantage on the board, always a step ahead of your opponents - or the opposite, you lurk just a step behind instantly taking advantage of the possibilities they open up.

A great game, I like it better with 2 players than I do Zimbabwe, with more I don't know (though I think I like Zimbabwe more due to its compressed abstracted and "purer" nature, but this is more accessible).

UPDATE: Zimbabwe is just a whole lot better than this, though this is still great

RATING: 6
Food Chain Magnate has a lot going for it. I like the variability of the modular board and the tremendous variety of paths that seem to be available to you. The theme is excellent and well integrated into the game. The rules come together in a surprisingly intuitive fashion.

But there are some limitations. Our first game was a whopping three hours, most of which was just watching the leader extend his lead. I don't mind that this is a "punishing" game, requiring good play to stay competitive, but there should be a way to hasten the end--or slow the leader--when an immutable blowout is underway. In addition, this game has the annoying and tiresome feature of the super-combo tableau of cards, in which someone chains together two dozen actions in a row while everyone else sits and watches. Finally, the calculation of who delivered where got old, repeatedly counting the same number of spaces between restaurants and houses, adding price cuts, checking Waitresses over and over again. I feel like this part of the game--and probably others--could have been streamlined.

In the end, Food Chain Magnate almost feels more like an "experience" game than a hard core strategy game I really want to sink my teeth into. It's sprawling and has a lot to explore. I kind of just want to have fun with it, but I'm not sure how much that's really possible.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9.6
FCM is both an abstract placement/route connection board game using tiles and an engine builder where you build up an organisation structure from company CEO downwards, recruiting new staff and training them though their career paths to develop their skills and effectiveness. The theme is that of food production, marketing & sales to meet demand either faster or at a lower price than other players. The game length is determined secretly at the start of the game and revealed once the bank goes bust for the first time. We played the longest game possible. I just love this game as everything you do impacts something else etc etc etc. I went for a lean mean organisation and only had 10 employees at the end and finished a close second in a 3 player game. Downtime with 4 player is a known issue. The sweet spot is apparently 3 players and the game is very brutal if you make errors. This stands out as my favourite Splotter game by a long way.

RATING: 10
This was my first Splotter game, and I wasn't really sure what to expect going in. First game was planned to be a full game, but we decided to call it shortly after the bank ran out the first time.

This game is excellent. It combines so many things I like into a single game, and while it can be a little long and fiddly, it really strikes a chord with me. The tech tree/deck builder hybrid that makes up the central core of this game works incredibly well, and I love how you have to balance all the different jobs in order to do well. I do think that the achievements can sometimes reward the rich a little too much for my tastes, but this does encourage repeated play and coordinated take-downs to improve your play.

My interest has been sparked, and I plan on checking out other Splotter games if I find any available.

UPDATE: Other Splotter games don't measure up to this one, in my opinion. That being said, I've played this more using the full game rules, and I have to admit that this has really blown me away. So many choices, and only a few can be made every round. The lack of any luck outside of the initial setup may ultimately be the one thing against FCM, but otherwise this game is amazing. Definitely a 10 in my book.

RATING: 6.5
You are the CEO of a restaurant. Each turn, you have to decide what type of people to hire, then you decide what type of beverage and foods to produce, then if you want to advertise and finally try to sell your food while undercutting the competition. 
Your org chart can only fit a certain size, and so you have to balance your restaurant size with paying salaries to expand. There are bonuses for achieving certain milestones, such as the person to hire the first waitress or first person to dump food to get a fridge.  The bonuses help you pay for things throughout the game.

The summaries are printed out like a menu, and the game components are nice.

However, it is not a very forgiving game. I've played twice, but never finished, since it seems to take around 4 hours to play. One person can get a lead such as by building  garden, and if people don't react properly there can be a runaway winner. Like The Great Zimbabwue, there is a calculation part of where you move your food to sell, and you have to count and optimize. I'm not fond of those games where you have to slow down and look around and start optimizing, especially if the graphics of the board are not entirely intuitive. Granted, you have to play a couple of times to understand roads and houses and blocks. But it is a bit of a chore to do that and it slows the game down. The Splotter Games generally get really good ratings, but they don't seem to be my type of game. On the other hand, I do like the idea of the org chart and how to try to make it as best as possible, so I like that aspect, but I dislike the selling of food phase. I'm willing to try again, but I have to be in the right mood for it.

RATING: 10
The gameplay is a 10.
The components are probably a 7 (great cards, ok board, terrible money)
Even so, I rate it a 10, because it's the only game that I can't stop thinking about after the gameplay ends. I just want to play again and again.

Strategy is deep. It may be a bit scripted at the very beginning, but the game is so long, that doesn't seem like a problem. Diversification of strategies come quick. You can trace every result back to a decision you made, good or bad.

The tech tree combined with area control is a great combination. I didn't think I would like the cutthroat nature of the game, but I turned out loving it.

RATING: 3.2
Just not my kind of thing. A graphic design that could charitably be described as perfunctory and at least twice as much bookkeeping and setup time as I like in my games are just the start of the problems here. More damning is a ruleset that only flirts with thematic consistency, leading to oddball outcomes (the way that marketing is handled is particularly strange, for example, as are the feast-or-famine results of the feeding phase). Ultimately the game seems to be centered around gaining player powers via reaching milestones and maximizing a (somewhat nonsensical) tech tree, so there's theoretically a lot of room for replay and depth of strategy. In practice, said tech tree just results in dozens of rules exceptions for each player to keep track of. Ultimately, the core round structure and range of options is fairly unappealing and not terribly fun, so I doubt I'll return for seconds.

RATING: 10
Awesome.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.2
While Splotter Spellen is not a company known by the aesthetics of its games -quite the contraty, in fact- I loved the fifties infography. The wooden pieces are cool. The board it's quite insipid but pragmatic. I only disliked the cardboard marketing tokens. The player aids in the form of long diner menus are just great. FCM is certainly the best looking Splotter “spiele”.

It's a heavy economic euro about managing a food company and competing with your rivals. It also presents you some goals or milestones that will tempt you to deviate from the ideal/planned strategy in order to obtain powerful effects that can change your game.

The core mechanism -and also the main attractive one- is how to manage your staff tree. You recruit, train, specialize, use, fire and pay them. Everyone has a special effect, allowing you to manipulate almost any area of the game.

Food Chain Magnate is a heavy euro game, but it's also very thematic. The mechanisms provide a believable abstraction about getting food and drinks, advertising them and selling them. The interaction is quite intense; right from the bat, you compete for the milestones that will produce key permanent (or instant) effects. A few turns into the game, you will start a fierce price competition with everybody until the very last turn. During all the game, you will also fight for the very important turn order.

There are many things to like about this game. My favorite feature is probably the many diverse strategies it promotes.

On the other side, it's a very long and quite unforgiving as well. Usually some players will find themselves out of the race early, and this game is not fun at all if you're not competing (there are other games where you can still have fun creating your engine or doing other things; not this one though). Also, every player chooses their prices secretly when choosing the active staff, and then everybody reveals them at the same time; this creates a random effect that doesn't fit well in such a heavy game. And my last complaint, I can see this game reduced to a price competition, about who can sell cheaper; in that case, it can drag a lot, because games are played until the bank runs out of money, and that can take a lot of time when players sell cheap.

First impression [url=https://boardgamegeek.com/geeklist/202797/item/4475127#item4475127]here[/url].

RATING: 8.5
Giocone della Splotter Spellen. Bello, entra tra i miei 3 preferiti della SS con Bus e Indonesia. Il gioco è più semplice del previsto come regolamento, anche se la spiegazione non è corta. Questi elementi rendono il gioco longevo e rigiocabile, anzi mette proprio voglia di rigiocarci spesso. Ci sono però un paio di cose che non mi gradisco, oltre alle imprecisioni sulle carte 'infinito' delle prime edizioni e il numero insufficiente di alcune Milestones (niente di realmente grave ma inspiegabili se paragonati al prezzo), trovo la grafica e il materiale di quello che dovrebbe essere il tabellone di gioco poco gradevole, così come alcuni componenti quali edifici e giardini. Carini invece i disegni sulle carte anni '50. Sulle meccaniche non ho grossi appunti, giocando in 5 giocatori si avvertono tempi morti che con l'esperienza comunque un po' si riducono. Trattandosi di un gioco della Splotter è destinato a rimanere in auge per molto tempo, il gioco ha sorprendentemente avuto già diverse ristampe. Il gioco ha vinto meritatamente il premio Magnifico 2016.

RATING: 10
Lo coloco porque empezamos a sacarlo a la mesa (dos partidas por ahora en el grupo). Aunque todavía estamos lejos de tener formada una opinión definitiva al respecto, ya lo veo saliendo muy seguido en las próximas reuniones, porque promete una experiencia extraordinaria.

En esencia, es un juego muy pro e implacable (unforgiving), donde hasta la primera decisión tiene enormes consecuencias para el resto de la partida. No sé cómo enfatizar ésto. Es pro e implacable al punto que Caylus parece un gateway en comparación. Por supuesto, en lo personal me resulta extraordinario, por más que nuestra última partida hayamos hecho cualquiera. Y no cualquiera a nivel de las malas decisiones que cometemos en las gameplay por acelerar el paso de la partida en vivo. Hablo de CUALQUIERA con mayúsculas, en el sentido de
"COMO NO LO VI!!! LRPMQLRMP!!!! "

Yo dupliqué al tercero, pero Lepo me ganó por 400 mangos (una tonelada de plata). Pero lo roto no era el juego, eran los jugadores.

Lo interesante es que pensándolo y conversándolo después, pudimos entender todos los caminos que podríamos haber tomado y qué infinidad de combinaciones te ofrece el juego para que cualquier jugador pueda encarar cualquier tipo de situación. Es una engine asombrosa.

Ese nivel de opciones estratégicas lo logra sin tanta complicación. Las reglas no son tan complejas para un juego de tan tremenda profundidad. Podés entender cómo jugarlo y jugarlo de una sentada si alguien te lo explica sin demasiado detalle. Claramente el desafío es dejar de ser horrible en tus decisiones, una propuesta que me atrapa completamente.

Si, la producción es meh en términos de componentes, pero esa engine tiene toda la pinta de una obra maestra.

Tengo que volver a jugarlo YAAAA

RATING: 7
There are some interesting dynamics re who's going to produce what next turn, who's going to meet what demand, and at what price ... which then help you decide whether you should be competing to meet the demand that other players have created through their marketing campaigns and beating them to the punch with lower price, or going your own way to create your own demands and meeting that instead for higher revenue. The game seems loaded with brain-burny if-then scenarios. At the strategic level, it seems to be one of determining how many turns the game will go and when's the best time to switch from investment (training up your mgt hierarchy) to revenue generation (with the best guys you've got) and will that revenue be enough to win the game when the bank runs out, and can you sustain that revenue once other people's big guns come out to play. Which can make for devastating end-game plays where a player thinks they'll be making money but get swamped out. This isn't a game for the feint-hearted, and can leave a bitter taste. Lots of interesting exploration options though.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Food Chain Magnate is Another heavy economic game from Splotter in which you're trying to produce and sell food and drinks to customers, subject to travel restrictions on a map board. More than anything else, though, it's an HR management game--most of the decisions revolve around deciding who to hire and train on a tech tree of labor skills. Players must not only produce the food items, but also use marketers to create demand for them. Other roles include managers that let you have more employees, HR people that let you hire more people, trainers that let your employees advance up the tech tree, and other miscellaneous roles that let you build more restaurant locations or even erect new houses for customers. The game rewards early action with a number of achievement cards that provide a permanent bonus, mostly for diving right into production, but investing in infrastructure early on may yield more flexibility down the road. It's definitely a difficult game, and you have to be quick to react when others create demand. I enjoyed this, but got caught flat-footed several times as the game state changed and I was powerless to adjust accordingly.

(1 play)

RATING: 6
I know it's hard to probably judge this game after only 1 play, but these are my findings:

In my 1 play I really enjoyed the first 2/3 of our game, I actually thought Splotter would finally have made a game I could really enjoy and was worth his high selling price. But the last 1/3 completely destroyed all the good feelings I had before. So a little reconstruction of our game:

First you have the inevitable race for the milestones, which is perfectly fine as you can chose to follow or to go for something different. But then the game sort of splitted up: where 3 players played 'positive' and already invested in advertisement and production in the hope to get some income, the 4th player sort of builded up a powerful engine, getting his sole income from waitresses, but for instance he could hire a person and upgrade it twice in the same turn, which gave him a lot of flexibility. So while the others created certain needs, he made a super factory, baked 8 pizzas and 8 burgers and collected all the drinks he wanted in a single turn and then positioned his restaurants cleverly so that he would fulfil all demands. By doing that, there was 1 full round where he simply cleared the board and made almost $ 150 as he had off course also a CFO. And the round after that he fulfilled 3/4 of the demands. We had played 2 hours at that point and everybody simply knew that player would win: as his card engine was by far better, he could anticipate to everything (if you would take a Discount Manager that gives - $3, he could simply do the same AND some other stuff), but the game still lasted for 1 extra hour and there was only $ 600 extra in the bank :s

That brings me to the 2 major concerns I have with this game:

1)  If you wanna win this game, you have to be the better leech. You simply have to let others make the efforts (like advertisement or building gardens) and then make sure you take the profits from their sweat. And off course you can argue: everybody can play like that, which is off course true, but then then you can ask yourself if that's the kind of game you wanna play? I certainly not.
2) For me this was the first game, but the others had played 3 more games in total. In all of these games there seemed to be a breaking point at a certain moment: 1 player who could cash in big time after which everybody knew he/she would be the winner. If the games end shortly after that, that's not an issue. But if the games then still lasts for so long you could easily play a fun game instead (like in our case still 1 hour, and I'm horrified by the thought there would have been $ 1200 in the bank reserve at that point :s), then for me that's a big problem. 


It certainly leaves me puzzled: the first 2/3 makes me intrigued about this game, wanna found out more. But the last 1/3 says 'keep it in shrink, the resell value will be higher'.

RATING: 8
To say this game is unforgiving is an understatement. Through no obvious fault of your own, other player's actions can effectively eliminate you from this game, and that makes for a terrible time. In my first play this happened to me and I was ready to get rid of it. People coaxed me into playing it a second time, and this time, it sang to me. It's a deep strategy game where you need to be carefully tracking not your opponent's current moves, but what moves they are lining up to make on their next turn. One mistake can cost you the game, and it's a wild ride while you fight tooth and nail to make money each turn and not let your opponents steal your customers out from under you. This is a game where one moment you feel perfectly safe and are doing well, and the next moment you are up against a wall, teetering on the brink of destruction. Absolutely work more than one play. The steep learning curve can be conquered. Don't get discouraged and you will be rewarded.

:star: Art, Iconography and components functional
:star: Clever or interesting mechanisms
:star: Reasonably low downtime between turns with mildly AP players
:star: Multiple plays stay interesting
:star: Low or mitigatable luck elements
:star::star::star::nostar::nostar: Raw enjoyment

RATING: 8.5
A really good game. I hesitate to go to "great" because it has all of the typical problems of a multiplayer game. Runaway leader, kingmaking, etc. And then the game tries to be a very serious strategy game, which it is. A lot of deep and fun systems. I actually think the theming and art is super fun. But the problem is that its a super serious game in this multiplayer free for all shell. That's actually what makes the game punishing and unforgiving. There are actually plenty of games with the strategic depth of FCM, they just aren't multiplayer FFA. That said, its still really fun and a lot of strategic depth.

UPDATE 10/10/17: Played my first 4 player game yesterday. Was really fun. The game is definitely on par with games like Blood Rage and IA, both of which I really enjoy. Very different reasons why I enjoy those games, but the level of enjoyment is similar. This game is much more difficult to understand and I'm still trying to understand a lot of the late game strategy. 

UPDATE 10/11/17: Played my 2nd 4 player game yesterday. One person did not have fun at all the entire game because she got so behind. The flaws of the game are showing again. I'm not a person who doesn't have fun when you can't do anything or you're losing... but quite a few people are, and I want to play with them. I like the strategy of this game, but it needs some more lightness in it so that strategy can be combined with just fun so that people of different stripes can play together. Maybe I'm returning to my roots of strategy dice chuckers where the dice mitigates the punishing aspects of the game, but where there can still be quite a bit of strategy.

RATING: 6
After one play of Food Chain Magnate I'm uncertain of exactly how I feel about this game. It seems immensely deep which I usually enjoy, but it seems very difficult to learn and do well. The game is mostly known information which I like, but there is so much stuff going on to keep track of that it was very hard to figure out what I should be doing, much less pay attention and try to predict what my opponents would be doing. 

The rules weren't that bad, but given all the effects of the various employees in the tech tree and the various achievements that are vital to the strategy of this game, it is pretty impossible to absorb them all during the first few plays. Alternatively to play well means to study and learn all those cards before making any decisions so you can lay out a plan. 

So all that is to say this game has a massive learning cure that means it really will only be fun when played among a group of fairly similarly experienced players. And actually given how zany the first few games can be with snowballing effects, it likely only really shines once you are playing with a group of experienced players. 

So the real question is was this game interesting enough to make me want to do that? And the answer is no. I liked it and will probably play again--but I will likely resign myself to the same fate that befell me in my first game--I will get crushed by the more experienced player with little idea of what to do better unless I put in the effort to really learn the game....and while I was more than happy to do so with other games, this one just feels more like work to get to that point. 

In the end I just don't find this game one that really makes me want to play it again and explore the depth it does offer.

RATING: 9.5
Excellent.  

The strategy is deep without being overly complex, and the modular, randomized map adds enough variance that the way to approach the game changes each time you play.  No single strategy is completely dominant, and there always seems to be a way to regain the upper hand on an opponent.  Doing so happens to be immensely rewarding, as there is no real element of chance.  

For example, flooding the board with demand for a product the opponent has difficulty producing can stop his or her strategy dead in its tracks.  Likewise, an opponent with dominant store positions can be undercut in price, creating a price war - and in the race to the bottom, a crafty player can increase demand and raise their own prices, thus making a killing on sales when opponents can't meet the volume of demand.

The flavor is unique and fun, and the retro 50's art style is fantastic.  I could go on and on, but I imagine you get the point.  

The only strike against this game is its length, which sits around 4 hours, but if game length isn't an issue for you and your group, then I highly recommend this one.    
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Pros:
1. Corporate Structure. This has a great mechanic of building out your employees from the top down. Poor planning on who you select as employees and how you build your structure can really hurt you.
2. Interaction. This game has great player interaction on the board. You can steal business from other players. You can move in to capitalize on another player's advertising. 
3. Brain Burner. This game is a super euro. Everything is deterministic. You will be thinking hard to puzzle out what to do.
4. Starting Locations. Where you choose to start your business has large implications on the rest of the game. This part of the game can be it's own little mini game.
5. First Hire Bonus. If you're the first player to get a certain type of employee down, you get a bonus. Other players can get the same bonus if they hire that employee in the same round. This creates a great dynamic of do you follow the path of the player a head of you to get the same bonuses or do you branch off to keep them from getting other bonuses.

Cons
1. Round Resolution. Resolving who gets to complete which orders can be extremely time consuming. You have to count out who's closes, where's the advertising, which restaurants produce, which routes are you taking to pick up drinks, what's the current demand. There's many things that interact on the board that can cause the resolution to be long and confusing.

Summary: This is a great brain burner of a game. It's fun to puzzle out how to boost your restaurants and steal demand from you opponents. It's one of the rare euros that actually hits a home run on having the mechanics fit the theme. Unfortunately this is a hard game to find for sale and it's fairly expensive. If you can get your hands on a reasonably priced copy. I highly recommend it.

RATING: 9.5
Truly excellent business game.  The rules are not complex, although it can take half a game to completely understand how demand is generated and how prices and distance work.

But it is also a game that can punish early mistakes, and they are easily made by the inexperienced or unwary.  In fact, it's very hard for any player to avoid making any mistakes; you just hope that your own mistakes aren't important ones!

I love the clear graphics and appearance of the game.

If it loses half a point anywhere, it's because putting tokens on the board then taking them off again can be a little fiddly; that a computer implementation that runs the marketing campaigns and mealtimes would be perfect (so long as it wasn't too fast for me to understand what was going on!).

A month later and another 6+ games and I remain firmly of the same opinion.  Except that I'm getting better at populating the map with tokens, and calculating which customers eat where.

Definitely my favourite business game.  Power Grid?  You're fired!

RATING: 8
Great engine building game with very good thematic integration of the mechanisms. You draft the actions you can do (i.e. hire personnel) and upgrade them (i.e. train your personnel). This upgrade-tree is the main engine of the game.

You first create demand for fast food and then compete with other players for the customers, by lowering the prices for your food. The board-tiles create a nice map with interesting topology.

You can earn extremely strong bonuses by achieving different tasks first (milestones). Because of this, the first games might be a bit hard for newcomers, since they can underestimate the engine building and the utility of these bonuses.

Update: After  about 5 face-to-face plays, most of which we did not finish, I lost a bit  interest in FCM, since  the physical handling of the components is extremely fiddly and time consuming (demand tokens, money, choosing which cards to play; adiministration of dinner time;  everything with new and kind of AP prone players is a bit too much then), even in comparison to other Splotter games as Indonesia. But then I played a few games online (boardgamecore) and I love the game. The spatial element, price wars, extreme swings; FCM has it all! The game is both tactical and strategic and you need both to win. 

The game  can play out extremely  differently: Sometimes players independently race to who can build an effective engine faster and other times it becomes highly interactive.

Two highly innovative mechanisms make this game a true masterpiece: First, the combination of price and distance to determine attractiveness for customers. Second, advertising of food instead of companies. The combination of both make the game highly interactive and interesting (I admire this as much as the merger mechanism in Indonesia). In consequence the game can become extremely mean as players undercut each other prices.

Even though I like the gameplay as much as Great Zimbabwe, I have to lower my rating a little bit due to handling problems of the physical implementation. Nevertheless, FCM is a great game.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 5

Since the milestones are removed from the game after somebody takes one of them, there is pretty much zero flexibility in strategy, you have to plan everything from the beginning, and since it is a Splotter game, you can ruin somebody's game if so wish.

The table space requirement is huge. Every player needs to have some sort player aid for the Milestones, thank god there is already one for the other cards.

RATING: 7
I'm not particularly a Splotter fan - I've played a few of their games, and have enjoyed most of them (Antiquity, Roads and Boats, Indonesia), yet without being wowed by any. I wouldn't have been particularly interested in FCM - but my wife decided she wanted it and bought it back in January or February. It arrived soon afterwards, and I read the rules, but never found a chance to play.

I finally got a game in on the last day of June - not with our copy, but with that of another member of our local games club, who I didn't even realise had it but had a game all set up when I turned up late that night. I was of course more than happy to play it, so there we go.

Ultimately my reaction was not dissimilar to the other Splotter games I've played - a very interesting design which I'd be more than happy to play again, but without feeling particularly drawn to it. It will certainly take many plays to get used to how all the various elements of the game interact, and find good strategies - and I can't imagine being unhappy to play this if the time is available. Yet nor do I feel I'd go out of my way to play again. I have reasonable hopes that this may grow on me in future plays however.

RATING: 4
A experiência foi horrorosa, terrível, dessas de ferir a dignidade. Não consegui fazer nada, não vendi nenhum lanche e terminei zerado.

Não quer dizer que o jogo seja horroroso, na verdade ele tem várias sacadas muito legais. Toda a mecânica do jogo é absurdamente temática, faz ele ficar fácil de aprender, tem muita interação, não tem um monte de regrinha e exceção, e é um jogo que vc sente que está jogando algo novo, diferente de tantos outros.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
[u]The positive[/u]
(+) The art: you hate it or you love it, I love it. The components are real good quality with nice colorful wooden food markers and different types of bottles and a huge amount of really nice cards. However, the map tiles could have been printer on thicker cardboard. The player aids which stand on the table as 'player's menu's' are great.

(+) This game bursts of interesting player interaction. Since marketing campaigns promote food and not brands of restaurants, players are offered so many interesting ways to approach marketing and ways to react on other players marketing effords.

(+) The action system is nicely integreted into building up your company structure with cards. This also offers plenty of opportunity to build up a successful company in many different ways: will you go for a small company mainly focussed on producing some goods and reaching some critical milestones early, or will you build up your company into a huge monster, losing some time in the process but gaining a lot of flexibility and offering some real strong action possibilities later on? Both strategies can be very powerful and there are multiple ways to approach both paths.

(+) The milestones: so many of them, and still so powerful. You can feel the tension at the start of the game when people are trying their best to snatch away as many of them as possible!

(+) At first, I feared the random map-tile placement would possibly result in uninteresting map layouts, but this concern proved to be unjustified. Thanks to the many crossroads, a limited amount of 'bridges' and a few special road layouts, the random way the city is built results in interesting layouts offering different strategies each time a new map is formed.

(+) Very structured and logical rules. It takes little efford to teach and understand the game

(+) The game offers some real satisfaction when after carefully planning and building up your company and working the market, you see money flowing in so you can train your staff to achive more powerful actions.

(+) The game flows rather smoothly and turns go by quickly, this game does not have much downtime. Action selection happens simultaniously and all the actions of a turn are resolved together one player after the other. Little to no downtime

(+) Detail but a sure positive: The box is just the right size to fit the huge amount of game components in there. Thank you Splotter for not taking up more space than necessary in our games closet!

[u]The negative[/u]
(-) One critical error could have devestating results on a player. The game offers no way to catch up again after you made a serious mistake, but it offers the competition multiple ways to completely and utterly destroy a player lagging behind. Personally, I don't mind an unforgiving game, but you risk this happining to a (new) player each time you bring this game to the table and you can be sure that this person will not have a nice evening, which also reflects on the others.

(-) You need a *lot* of table space. Our 6 persons dining table is barely large enough to fit all the different cards. Luckily, you lose some decks early in the game when milestones are reached.

(-) Game involves quite some bookkeeping at the end of each round when food and drinks get sold. For each house you have to count out distance and price, when gardens are present price gets doubled, but bonusses are not - all that sort of things tend to break the smooth gameflow a little.

[u]Tips:[/u] 
(*) Don't ever lose sight of the milestones early in the game
(*) Watch out when training staff: a level 2 card will require a salary and if something goes wrong and you don't earn enough money you will immediately lose the trained card (unless you reach the training milestone reducing salaries). Upgrading too many cards too early could be a critical mistake.
(*) Marketing is hugely important in this game. A player dominating marketing could bring havoc to others and huge revenues for himself.
(*) The first few turns are very important. Bring focus as to how you start up your company: small and fast to market and produce a certain type of food/drink and get you the marketing milestones and first burger/pizza produced or errand boy played or a firm in which you train and recruit more staff offering more powerful actions later on and granting more flexibility and milestones related to recruitment and training.
(*) Always watch for what the others are doing and build your strategy around that. Successful strategies depend on the map layout and the way others build up their food chain structures
(*) Price wars can be devestating. Watch out when a player gets the ability to produce a wide range of many products and then drops his prices.

[u]Conclusion:[/u]
Very intersting and smoothly flowing game which offers plenty of very intersting player interaction. Easy to teach and a reasonable game time compared to the contents offered. It offers different strategies depending on how the city gets put together and what paths opponents chose. It will satisfy heavy games solving the puzzle of efficently building up their company and playing the board correctly, but it will ruin players who lag behind or make the mistake of overshooting themselves too quickly.

Approx. game time: 45 - 55 min / player

RATING: 8.75
A game that is heavy due to the weight and power of every decision.  Like other Splotter games, this gives you an experience that doesn't feel like it's "on rails" in the way that most other games do.  You must think on your feet and predict the actions of opponents who are doing the same, and the effects of mistakes can be drastic.  This game should be played with players who will be interested, want to win, and are not the sort to get upset about the outcome, as runaway victories and huge swings are very possible.  This is not to say that this game can't be played casually.  However, if the attitudes of the players don't align, it's possible that the presence of a single player who plays to win and is not being "nice" will (even inadvertently) destroy the plans and potentially the experience of another player who is experimenting or playing casually.

RATING: 8
[2017-11-05]
Az Indonesia és az Antiquity után a legmagasabb helyezést elért Splotter játékot is volt alkalmam kipróbálni és ahogy a két eddigi játékukban ebben sem kellett kicsit sem csalódni. A játék teljesen egyedi nem is igazán tudom mihez hasonlítani. Célunk a kezdő kis éttermünket felvirágoztatni, esetleg új üzetlek nyitásával a kisváros hamburger, pizza, és három folyékony nedü igényét kielégíteni. Érdekes módon az igényekkel is nekünk kell foglalkoznunk a játékban, különböző kampányokat kell indítanunk, hogy a lakók érezzék, hogy ezekre nekik szükségük van.  Még a város házainak növelésével is kisérletezhetünk, hogy a mi bevételünk nőjön az új lakók beköltözésével. A tematika itt-ott azért nem tökéletes, mert pl. a pizza és a hamburger a semmiből keletkezik a szakácsaink által, míg az üdítőket a kifutófiún kívül a város lerakataiból szerezzük be. A játék két fő része közül az első a dolgozóink menedzselése. Renteg féle munkásunk lehet, akik fejlődőképesek is és egyre jobb tulajdonságokkal rendelkeznek később, ha képezzük őket. Vezetőként 3 dolgozót tudunk irányítani, de menedzserek segítségével később akár 20-30 főt is vezethetünk egyszerre. Rendkívül jó mechanizmus, hogy minden körben mi döntjük a dolgozóink közül, hogy kik jöjjenek az adott munkanapon dolgozni és ezzel a stratégiai mellett a taktikai is fontos szerepet játszik a társasban. A játék másik fő része a véletlenszerűen létrehozott térkép, amin nagyon fontos az üzletein jól elhelyezkedése, mert vásárlóink minél közelebb szeretnék elfogyasztani az ételeiket és italaikat, így ahhoz mennek, aki közelebb jobb áron árulja ezeket. Vannak mérföldkövek is a játékban, amik tulajdonképpen arra szolgálnak, hogy a teljesen egyforma kezdés után gyorsan különböző stratégiákat adjanak a kezünkben. Aki elsőnek teljesít valamilyen mérföldkövet, az további bónuszt kap. Nagyon ötletes a játék vége feltételének bekövetkezte is. Van egy pénzállomány, amit megszerezve még egyszer bekerül egy adott mennyiségű pénz a játékba, amit a játékosok úgy döntenek el, hogy titokban kiválasztják a 100-200 és 300 dolláros kártyák közül melyiket adják be és ha ez is elfogy váltja ki a játék végét. Abszolút több utas a játék, rendkívül fontos az időzítés. Talán egyetlen dolog nem tetszett benne, hogy van egy olyan mérföldkő, illetve dolgozó (igaz, őt elég nehéz kifejleszteni), ami, illetve aki azt tudja, hogy az adott körös bevételünket 50%-al megnöveli. Kár volt bele ez a runawayleader dolog. A végső ponthoz mindenképpen kell még egy játék, de előzetesen legyen 8-as.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
1 play, 4er

As usual for Splotter games, the rules themselves are not especially complicated -- it's all in the play. We used a blend of the intro and full games: $75x money in the bank, and with milestones. The intro game starts with $50/player in bank, the full game adds an additional variable amount as per players (secret) choices. The game ends when the bank is empty; the player with the most money wins.

Players start with one restaurant on the randomly constructed board. Each turn, players lay out their cards in an organizational hierarchy, with themselves (CEO) at the top and their employees below them (like a flowchart). You can send some people away on vacation, too. Employees can do a variety of things:
- managers can supervise other employees
- some can hire new people, or train those not currently working to higher-level employees
- improve your ability to collect various types of drinks (from sources on the board)
- cooks produce pizzas or burgers
- alter prices for goods sold
- advertisers create demand, on the NEXT turn, for the goods you produce
- etc.
Turn order adjusts every turn: more nimble companies (with more empty employee slots) go earlier. On your turn, all your employees act. The first player to do various things (e.g., first to place a billboard, first to produce food, first to play a waitress, etc.) gets a Milestone card: a permanent power useful for the rest of the game. Actually, everyone achieving that Milestone during the same game turn gets the benefit -- so you MUST keep an eye on everyone, make sure you are keeping pace.

After everyone has taken their actions, the houses on the board consume goods according to their current demand. They go to the restaurant that has exactly what they want; if multiple, then to the closest and cheapest. Restaurant owners earn for every food/drink sold. Then, everyone pays their employees.

Wow, such a neat game! It is, as usual, pretty unforgiving. There are a lot of gears to turn, and you can't get too far behind or it seems it would be impossible to catch up. I managed to play so poorly that I didn't get ANY milestones until quite late in the game ... DON'T DO THAT! As you'd expect, I was a distant last place. The game feels a bit like Antiquity: choose your own unique power, here based on the Milestones you go after; mostly you're playing on your part of the board doing your own thing, until you realize you can really screw with the other players by building in their direction (dump/pollution in Antiquity, marketing here); turn order manipulation (manned carts going later, vs unfilled employee slots going earlier) can be key at certain points in the game. I think there were similarities with The Great Zimbabwe as well, but I've only played that once (long ago) and don't remember the details.

Very good, tough, economic game! 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
This is a game of players competing to have the best fast food joint.  This is a nice, original theme which is what initially attracted me to the game.

The primary game mechanic is based on hiring and working with different types of workers.  Your workers do everything from advertise to cook food to open new restaurants to train your existing workers.  The rest of the game is based on map placement and management to ensure the residents of your sleepy 1950s town buy from your restaurant over your competitors.

This is a reasonably deep game.  There are lots of ways to get business over your opponents.  You can advertise and cook food that they don't, you can lower your prices, you can open restaurants closer to them, etc.  And by managing all these things properly you can even have your opponents do some of the work for you.  For example they can advertise a certain food product and by opening closer and cheaper to that household and making that product you just got the business your opponents worked to make them hungry for.

That's the part I liked the most.  How there were different ways to acquire business and distinguish yourself from the competition.  The rest of the game outside of the fun theme and multiple paths to fast food victory didn't sell me as much.  For one the game is incredibly unforgiving.  One mistake can pretty much hose you for the rest of the game.  At that point you're pretty much hoping your opponents also make a mistake but there's very little way to recover on your own otherwise and that can make for a pretty poor experience playing out the remainder of a 2-3 hour game.

The other thing I hate about games like this is by the final few turns there's not much you can do.  Especially on the final turn there's only a couple workers that will earn you money so you can be left with a handful of employees that do nothing for you.  This makes the end game a lot less interesting.

Finally, I have been very unimpressed with the quality of all the Splotter games.  Their printing is atrocious, with every cardboard chit off-center.  The minimal art design of this particular game might be excused as intentional style but really it just looks cheap and lazy.  This is especially inexcusable when you consider the high asking price for the Splotter titles.  The excuse I always hear when you bring this up about Splotter titles is that you're paying for the quality of the design not the quality of the product.  No!  That's not how it works.  You don't pay for the perceived quality of the design, you pay for the actual product you are buying.  I've never gotten a discount for seeing a shitty movie over an Oscar winner.  And that's even ignoring the fact that as of yet I've been pretty unimpressed with Splotter games.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
I love the unique theme of this game, but the game is just way to cut throat for me. The rules are simple, but the strategy is very deep. I love all the art and graphic design except for the map pieces, they were rather bland comparatively.

If you're making money it's not something special that you're doing. It's not that you decided to go different from what other players are doing, no! you're playing the game. And you're playing well. Why in the world should the player(s) getting 100$ receive a solid advantage after they already have reached a solid advantage in order to win?! It's the exact opposite of the so called catch up mechanism logic? Should we decide to split the game in two fronts, the others that got the milestone as a premier league for the game and the others as a first division trying not to get last? I am very confused about this.
