ONE.OF.THE.BEST.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Preliminary rating.

Seems like a wonderful evolution of ideas from earlier Splotter titles with some new twists too. Players control competing restaurant chains in a 1950s dystopian hell where the only food options are pizza, burgers and three kinds of beverage. Customers will buy based on a combination of price and distance, with demand dictated by various forms of advertising.

Competition is heavily dictated by turn order constraints, manipulation of price and manipulation of supply and demand; it's also important to build the company, however, and acquiring a burger chef now means deferring an opportunity to get, say, a HR manager for greater efficiency in future hires or a coach for training.

Key to strategy are the "milestones", overpowered bonuses for being the one of the first players to perform a task, such as a permanent +$5/pizza bonus (on a normal base price of $10) for being the first to advertise pizza. In practice, these work a lot like Zimbabwe's gods, but because they aren't so exclusive it's possible to jockey for them -- probably in exchange for passing up some other advantage.

If that sounds like it could lead to scripted play, board variability and player dynamics make that unlikely to have a problem. My only real concerns are about the difficulty keeping track of what other players have, but they're minor. A few plays in, Food Chain Magnate is looking like my game of the year.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7.25
Based on a single play. Will most likely change, potentially dramatically.

It is an interesting economic semi-engine building game. There is a lot going on and it is kind of a push and pull between other players doing supply and demand. My problems stem from the fact that the Milestones exist. They seem to almost force decisions in the first few turns and later (especially the $100 one granting a CFO) turn into a run away leader problem. Yes, it is possible to catch up, but getting 50% more money when you're the first to hit $100 is huge.

I think the game just might be better without milestones, although that might limit overall specialization and "first mover" decision making. Maybe some limitation of which milestones are in? Either way, it isn't a bad game, it is just different and overly complex.

Really enjoy some of the mechanisms (like the tech tree / hiring tree) but others (milestone, bank breaking, variable ending, and drink collection paths) seem a bit tacked on. Need to try it again with experienced players to make a full decision though.

EDIT: Have 2 more plays the game has definitely improved. When people know what they are doing the game is much better and more interesting. I question some of the balance choices of the game and the starting few turns feel scripted (due to the bonus "technology" cards you get), but it is still a very interesting title.

RATING: 10
Wonderful. I don't want to overstate my opinion, but the joy this game brings me is embarrassing. It can be painfully unforgiving and require a bind-bending amount of forethought to play well, but the delight I get out of both the theme and mechanics overcome what otherwise may be a daunting task. Most of all, it's the type of game that invites me to play it again and again: I want to unravel its mysteries and explore its depths. If that sounds overly poetic, I have no defense.

The game's strength is twofold. First, you have to plan your corporate structure out ahead of time. In this way, the game feels very much like a modern Euro. Hiring employees and building up your company feels vaguely like deck-building. While you never have to shuffle your deck, looking for synergistic combinations is a must. That said, comparisons with deck-building games are overstated. [i]When[/i] to hire an employee is just as important as choosing [i]which one[/i] to hire -- and that is very much dependent on not only your own strategy, but those of the other players as well.

For the second, and true strength of FCM is in the player interaction. Having a strategy of your own is important. Left unchecked, a player with a successful strategy can run away with the game, and quickly. But that's the key: you can't let anyone out of your sights. The game demands interaction. Otherwise, the whole thing can snowball out of control. If you don't believe me, try a two-player game against an experienced player. You won't know what you're doing, or how to stop the other person. You'll come away feeling like the game is yet another exercise in discovering and executing the perfect set-piece. But it's just not so! You have to play the second half of the game just as well as the first, and that means learning how to run interference with a vengeance. Plan, and then adapt. It's a tall order, but possible.

However, the game isn't perfect. Despite a bevy of options, there are only one or two opening moves that make sense long-term. Sometimes the random board is a bit lopsided. And of course you can find yourself in a situation where you've made enough mistakes that you're likely out of the running fairly early on. None of these flaws are enough to keep me away from the game. I suppose if you're the type of gamer who demands that all players have a chance of winning from beginning to end, you'll want to avoid FCM (for sure!), but that's not generally something I look for in my game selection.

Finally, what this game needs is a way to manage the ridiculously large footprint it requires in table space. Fans are already coming to the rescue with dry-erase boards and card holders, which I highly recommend. If all else fails, there is a very good online implementation.

If you are a fan of business games with high levels of interaction and room for multiple strategies (none of which can be be easily encapsulated because they are all dependent on the particulars of the board and actions of other players), then FCM is worth the entrance fee.

RATING: 9.7
This rivals Colt Express in terms of theme integration with mechanics, but does it in a much more complex and interesting way. There is so much depth to this game, both in the strategy and the theme. I feel as though every design choice was intricately weighed against the theme to see if it would not only function as a well oiled machine, but also tell a very particular story, and that it does. This game is a work of art. The art portrays a 1950's light slapstick take on the rise of corporate America through franchisation. This is what Monopoly aspired to be, a quirky insight into the pitfalls of capitalism. The game is cutthroat, demanding, selfish - all of the things it's theme encapsulates. The tropes are as clever as they are funny, and as eminent in the gameplay as well.

RATING: 7
Very good game! It feels very thematic and I feel very immersed in the fact that I am running a fast food chain. Also stays very true to the time the game takes place during. 

You have to play it with the right crowd that don't mind being able to lose in the very first round and having to play 3-4 hours more. Extremely cutthroat. 

So far I'm a little concerned that price reduction seems to be king. We've seen folks go for lots of waitresses or heavy training and market control, but usually the guy with the lower prices adapts pretty easily and just steals most of the market. Also some milesstones seem very strong and kinda lead openings in a certain way. Still though there are many many options and it's a great well made game!

RATING: 9
I don't like this game as a two player as the Guru/Radio strategy seems too strong.  As a 3-5 player game this is an incredible game.  

There should be a lot to delve into, a lot of paths to victory, and a lot of game state analysis. 

It has a tech tree which is always enjoyable.  It has nice tradeoffs.  A great theme, great graphic design, and excellent rules to theme matching.

I'd be surprised if this doesn't end up a 10, and a top 5 game for me.

This game has swings of who is controlling the game, which can cause massive amounts of frustration and hopelessness.  I tend to enjoy games that do this.  Other games I have felt "hopeless" is Tigris and Euphrates and Innovation.  Obviously, this is longer, more complicated, and shares no similar mechanics with the aforementioned.  It's just a similar feel when I'm losing.

This game really forces you to to pay close attention to the other players moves and potential moves because of these big swings.  This game has really made me notice how middle weight euro's tend to start out forcing you to pay attention to other players, but by the midgame you are locked into a strategy and there is too much inertia to do too much to other players plans without completely abandoning yours.  Food Chain Magnate is not like that (sometimes, the games can vary from session to session), which can make a session filled with frustration because it is filled with "I really should have seen that coming" moments.  Familiarity with game states and understanding the timing of the end game is critical.

There is a lot to love here.

RATING: 7
I've been interested in this game since the get-go. It's the latest Splotter game which immediately catches my eye. The theme and artwork was also very appealing to me. And the more I learned about how it played the more intrigued I was.

There are essentially two parts to the game. The first is a competition to advertise and sell fast food. There's a fierce competition to create demand on the map and then be the guy who can fill that demand. There's lots of ways to compete in that race, from advertising food, making food, competitively pricing food, moving/creating new restaurants to be closer to customers, developing new houses that might want your food, etc. This is the part of the game that really bends the brain.

The other half of the game and how you do all your stuff is related to your org chart. You hire, train and put to work all sorts of employees who do lots of different things. Every round you select the staff that will work that turn and they do their stuff.

The game starts with players selecting how much money will be put into the bank when it runs out of money the first time. This determines the length of the game. After the bank breaks, the new money comes in and the second time the bank breaks is the end of the game. You won't know how long the game will be until the reserve funds are revealed, so your strategy might not work with the game length.

Their are a ton of milestones, mostly for doing X first, where X could be one of a multitude of things. These usually give you some super cool bonus power that only you (or anyone who achieved it the same round as you) can do. This is a huge part of crafting your strategy in the game.

The game is tricky. You can be doing horribly, but manage to pull off a cool combo and suddenly surge into the lead. Or you can be set to have a great turn, but then have another player suddenly pull the rug out from under you by changing customer demand... or suddenly being able to serve your customers in your place. The opportunity to make big plays is potent, so planning ahead and having a massive plan come to fruition is potentially game-winning. I felt like there was a massive depth to the game that I couldn't begin to scratch. It's going to take several plays before I know how to navigate this game.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
About [thing=175914][/thing]:

[b]1) What is it?[/b]
[thing=175914][/thing] is an economic game of building a company in order to gain money by serving food and beverages to the people of the town. It is a game that requires players to make an engine capable of attending the needs of the people and creating them, using for this several workers.

The rules aren't all that hard to understand, but the game offers a lot to think about, going from positioning in the market, to hiring, training and putting the employees to work.

Once the few akward first steps are taken, the flow is smooth and everything connects well. All was left to worry the players is fierce competition in an overcrowded market, and all the paths [thing=175914][/thing] offers in order for you to became one.

[b]2) How do you play?[/b]
There is a lot to be said, so I won't. A really simplistic summary is: you will build a company. For this you start by hiring and training people. As a CEO you can oversee up to 3 workers. In order for your company to grow, it will be required more mid-level management, VPs and others - these can oversee more workers, making your company larger and more able to deal with several aspects of the food market: preparing food, buying supplies, marketing, developing new business, opening new venues and so on. And, especially early in the game, there will be competition for the milestones: the first to do some things (like the first to make a pizza "the original!", or to market a soda, or to put a waitress to work, and several others) will gain a nice bonus from that point on, and they can make a huge difference.

Players can only put to work as many people as they have "open slots" considering the amount of people the managers can handle. Each of them will activate during the player's turn, in the order chosen by the player. To train employees, they must be left out of the round (not used).

With the marketing campaings (that can be done by billboards, mail, airplanes and radio), the people of the town will start to want things - pizza, burguers, soda, lemonade and beers. Once they want something, they will go to the restaurant that can fulfill their need, no matter the distance, but they must be able to go there using the roads. Now, if two or more restaurants have what they want, then they will go to the cheapest, even this one is further away, but the price different drops 1 for every tile the family must travel, so they might decide to go with the costier just because is so much closer.

A family (household) can want more than one single thing, like burguer and lemonade. And will only go to a place that can fulfill all its needs or it will stay in the house and eat mac 'n cheese.

Players gain money by selling stuff (standard price: $10 per item), but the value can change due to milestones, princing managers, discount managers and luxuries managers. After dinner time phase (the selling phase) it is payday, and each worker that has a money icon on it must be paid $5 - it is possible to layoff people that you don't want to pay or can't.

Then the marketing campaigns, started earlier, affect the houses in their area of effect. After this, there is the clean up phase, when all the workers are returned to the players hands and the next round will begin.

The game proceeds like this until the money in the bank ends for a second time. The player with the most money will be the winner!

[b]3) Which are the decisions made during play?[/b]
Where to even start? There are loads of decisions made every turn and round. Going from work placement (using the employees) to positioning billboards around the city and what try to sell using them. To make it easy, there are four main and constant decisions to be made throughout to play:
a) Who to hire;
b) Who to train;
c) Who to put to work;
d) What to sell and at what price.

This may seem simple and it is, to understand, at least. Is a normal chain of business and everyone gets it. Still [thing=175914][/thing] gives a big set of choices within the choices - for instance, there are 32 differents workers! Plus considerations about slots to put workers, how to cut the competion, the best way to use the marketing, how to reach more households, to sell more, etc.

It is a very rich game regarding decisions to be made. And is all open to the players - there is no luck, events, surprises - except, of course, those made by the other players, which can sting much more.

[b]4) What are the good things in the game?[/b]
- Almost complete control by the players - highly strategic (As advertised: 100% dice free);
- So many paths to follow it reminds a food court;
- The allocation of the workers in a organizational chart is simple, effective and thematic;
- Great level of interaction between players;
- Random arrangement board ensure an even higher replay value;
- The graphic design for the cards and player aid are superb.

[b]5) Which are the bad news?[/b]
- Steep learning curve;
- Significant downtime, as players do all their actions in the Working phase one at a time, and considering that is in this phase most of the decisions are made, lots of time can be spent waiting;
- No catch up mechanism - if you lag behind, you will be close within the year.

[b]6) How do you feel while playing?[/b]
I'm in the food industry - with two partners I own a food truck. It is a small business, but several of the challenges faced in the game are the same we have: marketing to customers in strategic ways, pricing accordingly to the competition, and meeting demands. It emulates this challenging business smoothly and with some neat choices.

In [thing=175914][/thing], while you run from the macro point of view, there are parts of the business you won't have to deal with: no suppliers chain to negotiate and deal with; no concerns about safe work environment or legislation; no worries about standards or quality of ingredients, nor health issues; and I could go on. [thing=175914][/thing] isn't a simulation of the business. Is an economic game, that uses some extracts of the industry to create a frame to fill with theme and mechanics. And it did just that, and very well.

[thing=175914][/thing] is a heavy game, but most definitely not a dry one. It is filled with choices, competition and colors. The graphic design ties everything together and the mechanics, after some rounds, went into "shadow mode": you are playing, and not worrying about rules - you just want to sell some burguers and soda, godammit!, but those bastards of the Xango Blues Bar keep cutting your business with its waitresses and working almost at a loss, until they run you to the ground. Maybe its time to go to new regions, investing in regional manager and finding some houses with gardens to attend to. Notice? Players think in "real" names and options, not in rounds, phases, icons, etc.

Therefore, [thing=175914][/thing] goes very well recommended for those wanting a well done, heavy and complex game, with lots of heart, put in a not small playing time (around 3hrs). Not for everyone tastes, sure, but our brand director is working to make you hungry.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
I'm not sure if the tiny Splotter print runs will hold it back, but this game is an easy top 100 candidate if it manages to get any kind of circulation. It has the usual spartan graphics found in most Splotters, but the retro 50's theme of the game makes it a feature that feels completely appropriate. Gameplay is as deep as any other of the legendary Splotter releases I've played, but this one is much easier to slide into because of its unique deck-building/action selection system.

Basically, you draft a few cards every turn (which represent employees) and then play them in future turns to execute actions on the main board. These employees perform a wide range of actions in supplying, advertising and managing your business. You are limited on any given turn by how many executives you have on the payroll. The more supervisors you have, the more workers you can utilize. Of course those supervisors don't do much to advance the business on their own, so while you are training them up it is almost impossible to make sales on the main board. Stay small and you can generate fast profits serving whatever the local neighborhoods want. The trade-off for staying small is that you will never have the manpower to expand into new markets or create new ones.

Figuring out how to outmaneuver your competition is an absolutely fascinating puzzle. Sell cheap? Offer a full service wait staff? Ad-bomb a neighborhood and turn them onto a completely new offering? Every one of these can be effective and every one can be countered by a savvy opponent. Yet the game never gets complicated. The actions are very straightforward with minimal rules overhead. The game also has a nice strategic arc. Early turns are simple and easy to plan, with each newer turn growing a little more intense. As the game turns the corner into the final rounds, it becomes an agonizing chess match trying to lure customers away from your opponent's establishments. Just when your organization is on the verge of becoming to large to handle, the game ends. It's damn near a perfect play length.

RATING: 7.5

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 3
This is the most fiddly game I've ever played, if anyone calls Sentinels Of The Multiverse fiddly again I will hit them in the head with this game's box as many times as the furthest diner's price + distance would be to house number 15 if the house has gardens and there is a discount manager at work but also a luxury manager but no counting the bonus from that milestone you got last turn. So as you see the way you have to count distance on a weird map and add prices which have modifiers to it to determine where people go ... for every single house is tiring as hell. It also makes the game either unplannable or headhurtingly AP-prone
The tech tree is the interesting part of the game but that takes up insane ammount of table space and is again fiddly as hell. What really makes me dislike this game though is how advertising works. In a game this detailed the fact that your ads don't sell your products at all, they just generate demand for a type of stuff anyone can fulfill ... which not only is unthematic for me, but from a pure mechanism standpoint it is a hotbed of kingmaking. For example I got most of my points because someone mistakenly (because - again - it would be a chore to count distances and shit) advertised in a way that was good for me and not for him. That is not elegant design at all. I kind of understand why this "do whatever" kind of sandbox is appealing to people but for me it's neither good design nor a good experience.

RATING: 9
One of the most demanding and unforgiving games I've ever played. And although there are some things about it that I'm not particularly fond of (e.g. it can suffer from runaway leader problem), overall this is an amazing design. There's absolutely zero luck here, everything depends purely on your strategy. I love the tight competition over the customers and the complex decision-making. Obviously it's not a perfect economic simulation, but the HR and marketing aspects are handled extremely well. This game just oozes theme! I love it.

The retro-look of the game is fantastic. Some might find it to be too simplistic (to the point of being prototype-like), but I think that the graphic design just fits the theme perfectly.

RATING: 8
I haven't played a "full" game of this yet, but the more I play it, the more I like it. In the game, you're building org charts, producing marketing campaigns (to drive demand of goods), and then satisfying demand (to make money).

This was the first game where we paid salaries and used reserve cards, before I had only played short games. However, we still didn't play with milestones yet and I'm glad we left them out for now. In this game, it felt a bit one sided as I managed to get a lot of the good one-of cards and used them to really push into the lead.

Tentatively recommended, and even if I had a few games under my belt, this isn't for everyone.

RATING: N/A
Odd and heavy card driven euro on a modular city board to grow your fast food empire in the city. Build your org chart of hired personnel cards with special abilities from trainees, short order cooks, truck drivers, marketing staff, recruiting personnel. Those not on the job are at the beach. Meanwhile, you are running a multi-media advertising campaign to generate demand for your menu from planes, mailboxes, radio, & billboards. Produce the food to meet the campaign driven demand depending on the different range logistics of the ad programs. Customers are also price and distance sensitive. Any wasted food goes to the freezer or thrown out. Make payroll, move restaurants to better locations, new houses of demand pop up here and there... Woof. A lot going on. Retro '50's themed game look and feel. Love these guys and there game niche is so unique in theme and mechanics...but... I have trouble grasping and explaining these games, so I don't get them to the table often enough. Mad respect though. A cutthroat longish game involving many systems where everyone's strategy is played out in the open to be the first to trigger the victory condition by breaking the bank for mad stacks of cash yo.

RATING: 8.24
This is a cut-throat business game; you will cook,market and sell food items to homes on a map. You will need to recruit from a wide variety of potential employees, mostly cooking staff, marketing staff and management/HR staff. You will also want to develop the city board by placing advertisements, building new restaurants and commissioning new homes to be built.  You may find a niche in the market and make a ton of money by raising prices, or you might get undercut by an opponent and make nothing.  This is not a game for playing nice.

RATING: 10
Theme/Art:
The theme is great, running a fast food chain is pretty unique and fun. The art is not the best I have ever seen (the map looks pretty bland and boring), but the card designs fit the theme well.

Production quality:
Very solid. The game consists of a lot of cards (which feel nice), cardboard tiles and tokens, and wooden resources.

Replayability:
The map is set up randomly, which makes every game different. The secret bank cards determine the length of the game, and you can go for quite a few strategies with the amount of different employees and milestones.

Depth/Difficulty:
It's not a difficult game, it's actually quite easy and straight forward, but you have to know what you are doing and keep track of your components and adapt to their plays.
I weight it 4/5

Strategy:
You need to know what you are doing, what you are going for, and how you will prevent your opponents from interfering with your plans. I also strongly advise that you play with people of equal skill, because this game is VERY unforgiving. If nobody knows what they are doing it should be fine, if everybody knows the game, it should also be fine, but if one knows if and the others don't there is no way they can win, because at first you don't realise how important milestones are and how quickly you can change where all the people on the map eat.

Luck:
Basically none, aside from the randomly generated map.


Food Chain Magnate takes about 1 hour per player and is an absolutely fantastic game. I love how you have to create your own market by advertising goods, only to find out another player sells the same for a lower price and you are unable to sell your burgers.

RATING: 8
This game is good enough to warrant the reputation it's built. I liken it a bit to The Gallerist -- both of them are quite easy to actually play, but to play them WELL is a whole different story. I love the little wooden bits here (and there are a lot of them!). The player menus were a great idea, and they're super useful. Each phase is laid out step by step on the front, all the employee types in the middle, and all the milestones and marketing info on the back. Thematically, all the employee types make sense except for one (why would a zeppelin pilot be able to pick up drinks?!) and that's awesome - it really feels like you're building a company.  For downsides, #1 is paper money. I HATE paper money.  #2, even though there are lots of employee cards, I feel like there's going to be a "strategy to rule them all" in the beginning, and each game won't start to feel differently until strategies diverge a bit after most of the milestones are gone. This has been true in all my plays so far -- it might be group think, but a little research reveals the same ideas.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Main game mechanics are fabulous and extremely thematic, e.g. the hiring, the corporate structure, the choices consumers make, the price cutting etc. Player interaction is high, though mostly indirect. 

The choosing of bank reserve cards at the start of the game seems odd as the game requires players to adapt to one another's actions from turn to turn. There is a lot of mental calculation required when considering the state of the board and this leads to substantial downtime. More calculations are required during resolution of dinnertime, which bogs down the game. There are no catch-up mechanisms, so once a player is behind, it is difficult to recover. Some time is also required at the start of the game for the tension to build up.

Most of the artwork are nice, but those of the main board and the advertising boards are rather plain. Downtime can be long, particularly in the initial games and with more players. Game component quality is above average.

RATING: 9.5
Wow is this game good.  Of all the games I have played, this does the most with the least.  The rules are pretty simple and straight forward (minus some translation ambiguity that is easy amended by reading the forums and errata), but this game hard to actually play well.

The game can be brutal if you mess up and small miscues in your short-term tactical or long term strategy will be costly.

But man, is it fun to play around with.  Such an open, nonlinear game that allows you to execute many different types of strategies... it's really a rare game that allows you this much flexibility to explore and discover each play (and be ruthless to your opponents!)

RATING: 10
Hay juegos que los miras y te devuelven la mirada, y ya sabes que tienes que comprarlos. Food Chain Magnate es uno de ellos. Un vistazo a la portada ya te dice que lo que guarda en su interior va a ser muy especial. 

¿Quién no ha querido alguna vez ponerse esa horrible gorra de vendedor de hamburguesas y lanzarse al mercado medrando en el mundo de las cadenas de comida rápida? Los meeples de refrescos tienen la forma de las "Nuka-Kolas" al más puro estilo Fallout, los personajes que pululan por las ilustraciones llevan esa acartonada alegría preholocausto nuclear tan típicamente americana, incluso existen recompensas en la línea de los Beneficios del videojuego.

 Aquí no se trata de matar mutantes sino de hacerse con el corazón de los clientes, creciendo sin medida y amasando cantidades ingentes de pasta - de pizza y dinero -. Si aderezas las partidas con algo de jazz la experiencia ya será sublime, y si es sacada de tu Pip-Boy 3000 mejor que mejor.

RATING: N/A
Purchased after watching the full play-through by Heavy Cardboard, reading the (mostly) stellar reviews, and finding out that there was one last copy at [url=http://www.milsims.com.au/]Milsims[/url].

[b]1st play, 3p[/b]:

Spoiler: It really lived up to the hype. While there are some fiddly rules to remember, it isn't a particularly difficult game to learn, but I suspect it will take you some plays to find out what to focus on during the early (answer: milestones!), mid and late game. There is also a lot of potential for player aggression through the form of manipulating demand and by placing new restaurants, forcing others to balance price and supply to overcome those hurdles. The org chart (a different take on action selection I suppose) is a mechanic I'd love to see in future games.

Initial rating: [b]8.5[/b]

[b]Bonus[/b]: Here's a couple of things we did wrong:

1) "Cards reporting to a manager can only be normal employees, not other managers." We actually ended up with a huge tech tree.
2) [after the bank breaks for the first time] "Whichever number occurs most determines the number of slots all CEO’s will have from the next turn onwards."
3) "... if you have a radio campaign running in phase 6: place 2 goods of the type advertised..."
